years</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-11"><a href="#Emperor"><span class="tocnumber">3</span> <span class="toctext">Emperor</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-12"><a href="#Accession_of_Marcus_Aurelius_and_Lucius_Verus_(161)"><span class="tocnumber">3.1</span> <span class="toctext">Accession of Marcus Aurelius and Lucius Verus (161)</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-13"><a href="#Early_rule"><span class="tocnumber">3.2</span> <span class="toctext">Early rule</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-14"><a href="#War_with_Parthia_(161&#8211;166)"><span class="tocnumber">3.3</span> <span class="toctext">War with Parthia (161&#8211;166)</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-15"><a href="#War_with_Germanic_tribes_(166&#8211;180)"><span class="tocnumber">3.4</span> <span class="toctext">War with Germanic tribes (166&#8211;180)</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-16"><a href="#Legal_and_administrative_work"><span class="tocnumber">3.5</span> <span class="toctext">Legal and administrative work</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-17"><a href="#Plague"><span class="tocnumber">3.6</span> <span class="toctext">Plague</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-18"><a href="#Death_and_succession_(180)"><span class="tocnumber">3.7</span> <span class="toctext">Death and succession (180)</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-19"><a href="#Legacy_and_reputation"><span class="tocnumber">4</span> <span class="toctext">Legacy and reputation</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-20"><a href="#Attitude_towards_Christians"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">Attitude towards Christians</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-21"><a href="#Marriage_and_children"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">Marriage and children</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1"><a href="#Nerva&#8211;Antonine_family_tree"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Nerva&#8211;Antonine family tree</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-22"><a href="#Writings"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">Writings</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-23"><a href="#Equestrian_Statue_of_Marcus_Aurelius"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">Equestrian Statue of Marcus Aurelius</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-24"><a href="#Column_of_Marcus_Aurelius"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">Column of Marcus Aurelius</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-25"><a href="#Notes"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">Notes</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-26"><a href="#Citations"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">Citations</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-27"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">13</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-28"><a href="#Ancient_sources"><span class="tocnumber">13.1</span> <span class="toctext">Ancient sources</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-29"><a href="#Modern_sources"><span class="tocnumber">13.2</span> <span class="toctext">Modern sources</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-30"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">14</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sources">Sources</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Marcus_Aurelius&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Sources">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Marcus_Aurelius_bust_Istanbul_Archaeological_Museum_-_inv._5129_T.jpg"><img alt="Bust of Marcus Aurelius" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="5181" data-file-width="3454" decoding="async" height="330" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Marcus_Aurelius_bust_Istanbul_Archaeological_Museum_-_inv._5129_T.jpg/220px-Marcus_Aurelius_bust_Istanbul_Archaeological_Museum_-_inv._5129_T.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Marcus_Aurelius_bust_Istanbul_Archaeological_Museum_-_inv._5129_T.jpg/330px-Marcus_Aurelius_bust_Istanbul_Archaeological_Museum_-_inv._5129_T.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Marcus_Aurelius_bust_Istanbul_Archaeological_Museum_-_inv._5129_T.jpg/440px-Marcus_Aurelius_bust_Istanbul_Archaeological_Museum_-_inv._5129_T.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Marcus_Aurelius_bust_Istanbul_Archaeological_Museum_-_inv._5129_T.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bust of Marcus Aurelius in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Archaeological_Museum_of_Istanbul" title="Archaeological Museum of Istanbul">Archaeological Museum of Istanbul</a>, Turkey</div></div></div>\n<p>The major sources depicting the life and rule of Marcus are patchy and frequently unreliable. The most important group of sources, the biographies contained in the <i><a href="/wiki/Augustan_History" title="Augustan History">Historia Augusta</a></i>, claim to be written by a group of authors at the turn of the 4th century AD, but were in fact written by a single author (referred to here as \'the biographer\') from about 395 AD. The later biographies and the biographies of subordinate emperors and usurpers are unreliable, but the earlier biographies, derived primarily from now-lost earlier sources (<a href="/wiki/Marius_Maximus" title="Marius Maximus">Marius Maximus</a> or Ignotus), are much more accurate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Birley,_1889_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Birley,_1889-3">[3]</a></sup> For Marcus\' life and rule, the biographies of <a href="/wiki/Hadrian" title="Hadrian">Hadrian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Antoninus_Pius" title="Antoninus Pius">Antoninus</a>, Marcus, and <a href="/wiki/Lucius_Verus" title="Lucius Verus">Lucius</a> are largely reliable, but those of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aelius_Verus" title="Aelius Verus">Aelius Verus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Avidius_Cassius" title="Avidius Cassius">Avidius Cassius</a> are not.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup>\n</p><p>A body of correspondence between Marcus\' tutor <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Cornelius_Fronto" title="Marcus Cornelius Fronto">Fronto</a> and various Antonine officials survives in a series of patchy manuscripts, covering the period from c. 138 to 166.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> Marcus\' own <i>Meditations</i> offer a window on his inner life, but are largely undateable and make few specific references to worldly affairs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> The main narrative source for the period is <a href="/wiki/Cassius_Dio" title="Cassius Dio">Cassius Dio</a>, a Greek senator from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bithynian" title="Bithynian">Bithynian</a> <a href="/wiki/Nicaea" title="Nicaea">Nicaea</a> who wrote a history of Rome from its founding to 229 in eighty books. Dio is vital for the military history of the period, but his senatorial prejudices and strong opposition to imperial expansion obscure his perspective.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> Some other literary sources provide specific details: the writings of the physician <a href="/wiki/Galen" title="Galen">Galen</a> on the habits of the Antonine elite, the orations of <a href="/wiki/Aelius_Aristides" title="Aelius Aristides">Aelius Aristides</a> on the temper of the times, and the constitutions preserved in the <i><a href="/wiki/Digest_(Roman_law)" title="Digest (Roman law)">Digest</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Codex_Justinianus" title="Codex Justinianus">Codex Justinianus</a></i> on Marcus\' legal work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Epigraphy" title="Epigraphy">Inscriptions</a> and <a href="/wiki/Numismatics" title="Numismatics">coin finds</a> supplement the literary sources.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life_and_career">Early life and career</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Marcus_Aurelius&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Early life and career">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Early_life_of_Marcus_Aurelius" title="Early life of Marcus Aurelius">Early life of Marcus Aurelius</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Young_Marcus_Aurelius_Musei_Capitolini_MC279.jpg"><img alt="Bust of a young Marcus Aurelius" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3333" data-file-width="2500" decoding="async" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Young_Marcus_Aurelius_Musei_Capitolini_MC279.jpg/220px-Young_Marcus_Aurelius_Musei_Capitolini_MC279.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Young_Marcus_Aurelius_Musei_Capitolini_MC279.jpg/330px-Young_Marcus_Aurelius_Musei_Capitolini_MC279.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Young_Marcus_Aurelius_Musei_Capitolini_MC279.jpg/440px-Young_Marcus_Aurelius_Musei_Capitolini_MC279.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Young_Marcus_Aurelius_Musei_Capitolini_MC279.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A bust of young Marcus Aurelius (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Capitoline_Museum" title="Capitoline Museum">Capitoline Museum</a>). <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Birley" title="Anthony Birley">Anthony Birley</a>, his modern biographer, writes of the bust: \'This is certainly a grave young man.\'<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Name">Name</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Marcus_Aurelius&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Name">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Marcus was born in <a href="/wiki/Rome" title="Rome">Rome</a> on 26 April 121. His name at birth was supposedly Marcus Annius Verus,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> but some sources assign this name to him upon his father\'s death and unofficial adoption by his grandfather, upon his coming of age,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Historia_MA_I.9&#8211;10_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Historia_MA_I.9&#8211;10-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._33_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._33-14">[14]</a></sup> or at the time of his marriage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> He may have been known as Marcus Annius Catilius Severus,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> at birth or at some point in his youth,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Historia_MA_I.9&#8211;10_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-Historia_MA_I.9&#8211;10-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._33_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._33-14">[14]</a></sup> or Marcus Catilius Severus Annius Verus. Upon his adoption by Antoninus as heir to the throne, he was known as Marcus Aelius Aurelius Verus Caesar and, upon his ascension, he was Marcus Aurelius Antoninus Augustus until his death;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Epiphanius_of_Salamis" title="Epiphanius of Salamis">Epiphanius of Salamis</a>, in his chronology of the Roman emperors <i><a href="/wiki/On_Weights_and_Measures" title="On Weights and Measures">On Weights and Measures</a></i>, calls him <i>Marcus Aurelius Verus</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Family_origins">Family origins</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Marcus_Aurelius&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Family origins">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Marcus\' family originated in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Espejo,_C%C3%B3rdoba" title="Espejo, C&#243;rdoba">Ucubi</a>, a small town southeast of <a href="/wiki/C%C3%B3rdoba,_Spain" title="C&#243;rdoba, Spain">C&#243;rdoba</a> in Iberian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baetica" title="Baetica">Baetica</a>. The family rose to prominence in the late 1st century AD. Marcus\' great-grandfather Marcus Annius Verus (I) was a <a href="/wiki/Roman_Senate" title="Roman Senate">senator</a> and (according to the <i>Historia Augusta</i>) ex-<a href="/wiki/Praetor" title="Praetor">praetor</a>; his grandfather <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Annius_Verus_(II)" title="Marcus Annius Verus (II)">Marcus Annius Verus (II)</a> was made a <a href="/wiki/Patrician_(ancient_Rome)" title="Patrician (ancient Rome)">patrician</a> in 73&#8211;74.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> Through his grandmother <a href="/wiki/Rupilia" title="Rupilia">Rupilia</a>, Marcus was a member of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nerva-Antonine_dynasty" title="Nerva-Antonine dynasty">Nerva-Antonine dynasty</a>; <a href="/wiki/Salonia_Matidia" title="Salonia Matidia">Salonia Matidia</a> &#8211; the emperor <a href="/wiki/Trajan" title="Trajan">Trajan</a>\'s niece through <a href="/wiki/Ulpia_Marciana" title="Ulpia Marciana">his sister</a> &#8211; was the mother of Rupilia and her half-sister, Hadrian\'s wife <a href="/wiki/Vibia_Sabina" title="Vibia Sabina">Sabina</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[notes 1]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Verus\' and Rupilia\'s elder son &#8211; Marcus\' father &#8211; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marcus_Annius_Verus_(III)" title="Marcus Annius Verus (III)">Marcus Annius Verus (III)</a> married <a href="/wiki/Domitia_Lucilla" title="Domitia Lucilla">Domitia Lucilla</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[24]</a></sup> She was the daughter of the patrician P. Calvisius Tullus Ruso and Domitia Lucilla, who had inherited a great fortune (described at length in one of <a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Younger" title="Pliny the Younger">Pliny</a>\'s letters) from her maternal grandfather and her paternal grandfather by adoption.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[25]</a></sup> The younger Lucilla would acquire much of her mother\'s wealth, including a large brickworks on the outskirts of Rome &#8211; a profitable enterprise in an era when the city was experiencing a construction boom.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[26]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Childhood">Childhood</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Marcus_Aurelius&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Childhood">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Marcus\' sister, <a href="/wiki/Annia_Cornificia_Faustina" title="Annia Cornificia Faustina">Annia Cornificia Faustina</a>, was probably born in 122 or 123.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[27]</a></sup> His father probably died in 124, during his praetorship, when Marcus was three years old.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_31_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_31-29">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[notes 2]</a></sup> Though he can hardly have known his father, Marcus wrote in his <i>Meditations</i> that he had learned \'modesty and manliness\' from his memories of his father and from the man\'s posthumous reputation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[30]</a></sup> His mother Lucilla did not remarry<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_31_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_31-29">[28]</a></sup> and, following prevailing aristocratic customs, probably did not spend much time with her son. Instead, Marcus was in the care of \'nurses\'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[31]</a></sup> Even so, he credits his mother with teaching him \'religious piety, simplicity in diet\', and how to avoid \'the ways of the rich\'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[32]</a></sup> In his letters, he makes frequent and affectionate reference to her; he was grateful that, \'although she was fated to die young, yet she spent her last years with me\'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[33]</a></sup>\n</p><p>After his father\'s death, Marcus was raised by his grandfather Marcus Annius Verus (II), who had always retained the legal authority of <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Patria_potestas" title="Patria potestas">patria potestas</a></i> over his son and grandson. Technically this was not an adoption, the creation of a new and different <i>patria potestas</i>. <a href="/wiki/Lucius_Catilius_Severus" title="Lucius Catilius Severus">Lucius Catilius Severus</a>, described as Marcus\' maternal great-grandfather, also participated in his upbringing; he was probably the elder Domitia Lucilla\'s stepfather.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._33_14-2"><a href="#cite_note-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._33-14">[14]</a></sup> Marcus was raised in his parents\' home on the <a href="/wiki/Caelian_Hill" title="Caelian Hill">Caelian Hill</a>, which he would affectionately refer to as \'my Caelian\'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[34]</a></sup> It was an upscale area with few public buildings but many aristocratic villas. Marcus\' grandfather owned a palace beside the <a href="/wiki/Lateran_Palace" title="Lateran Palace">Lateran</a>, where he would spend much of his childhood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[35]</a></sup> Marcus thanks his grandfather for teaching him \'good character and avoidance of bad temper\'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[36]</a></sup> He was less fond of the mistress his grandfather took and lived with after the death of his wife Rupilia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._35_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._35-39">[37]</a></sup> Marcus was grateful that he did not have to live with her longer than he did.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[38]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Marcus was educated at home, in line with contemporary aristocratic trends;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[39]</a></sup> he thanks Catilius Severus for encouraging him to avoid public schools.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[40]</a></sup> One of his teachers, Diognetus, a painting master, proved particularly influential; he seems to have introduced Marcus Aurelius to the philosophic way of life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[41]</a></sup> In April 132, at the behest of Diognetus, Marcus took up the dress and habits of the philosopher: he studied while wearing a rough <a href="/wiki/Pallium_(Roman_cloak)" title="Pallium (Roman cloak)">Greek cloak</a>, and would sleep on the ground until his mother convinced him to sleep on a bed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[42]</a></sup> A new set of tutors &#8212; <a href="/wiki/Alexander_of_Cotiaeum" title="Alexander of Cotiaeum">Alexander of Cotiaeum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Trosius_Aper" title="Trosius Aper">Trosius Aper</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tuticius_Proculus" title="Tuticius Proculus">Tuticius Proculus</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[notes 3]</a></sup> &#8212; took over Marcus\' education in about 132 or 133.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[44]</a></sup> Little is known of the latter two (both teachers of <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a>), but Alexander was a major litt&#233;rateur, the leading <a href="/wiki/Homer" title="Homer">Homeric</a> scholar of his day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[45]</a></sup> Marcus thanks Alexander for his training in literary styling.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[46]</a></sup> Alexander\'s influence &#8211; an emphasis on matter over style and careful wording, with the occasional Homeric quotation &#8211; has been detected in Marcus Aurelius\' <i>Meditations</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[47]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Succession_to_Hadrian">Succession to Hadrian</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Marcus_Aurelius&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Succession to Hadrian">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:AELIUS_CAESAR_RIC_II_987-671493.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="144" data-file-width="325" decoding="async" height="133" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/AELIUS_CAESAR_RIC_II_987-671493.jpg/300px-AELIUS_CAESAR_RIC_II_987-671493.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/8d/AELIUS_CAESAR_RIC_II_987-671493.jpg 1.5x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:AELIUS_CAESAR_RIC_II_987-671493.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Roman_currency" title="Roman currency">Coin</a> (136&#8211;138 AD) of <a href="/wiki/Hadrian" title="Hadrian">Hadrian</a> (obverse) and his adoptive son, <a href="/wiki/Lucius_Aelius" title="Lucius Aelius">Lucius Aelius</a> (reverse). Hadrian is wearing the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Laurel_crown" title="Laurel crown">laurel crown</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>In late 136, Hadrian almost died from a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hemorrhage" title="Hemorrhage">hemorrhage</a>. Convalescent in <a href="/wiki/Hadrian%27s_Villa" title="Hadrian\'s Villa">his villa</a> at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tivoli,_Italy" title="Tivoli, Italy">Tivoli</a>, he selected Lucius Ceionius Commodus, Marcus\' intended father-in-law, as his successor and adopted son<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[48]</a></sup> <i>invitis omnibus</i>, \'against the wishes of everyone\'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[49]</a></sup> While his motives are not certain, it would appear that his goal was to eventually place the then-too-young Marcus on the throne.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[50]</a></sup> As part of his adoption, Commodus took the name Lucius Aelius Caesar. His health was so poor that, during a ceremony to mark his becoming heir to the throne, he was too weak to lift a large shield on his own.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[51]</a></sup> After a brief stationing on the <a href="/wiki/Danube" title="Danube">Danube</a> frontier, Aelius returned to Rome to make an address to the senate on the first day of 138. The night before the speech, however, he grew ill, and died of a hemorrhage later in the day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[notes 4]</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 24 January 138, Hadrian selected Aurelius Antoninus, the husband of Marcus\' aunt <a href="/wiki/Faustina_the_Elder" title="Faustina the Elder">Faustina the Elder</a>, as his new successor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[54]</a></sup> As part of Hadrian\'s terms, Antoninus in turn adopted Marcus and Lucius Commodus, the son of Lucius Aelius.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[55]</a></sup> Marcus became M. Aelius Aurelius Verus, and Lucius became L. Aelius Aurelius Commodus. At Hadrian\'s request, Antoninus\' daughter Faustina was betrothed to Lucius.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[56]</a></sup> Marcus reportedly greeted the news that Hadrian had become his adoptive grandfather with sadness, instead of joy. Only with reluctance did he move from his mother\'s house on the Caelian to Hadrian\'s private home.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[57]</a></sup>\n</p><p>At some time in 138, Hadrian requested in the senate that Marcus be exempt from the law barring him from becoming <i><a href="/wiki/Quaestor" title="Quaestor">quaestor</a></i> before his twenty-fourth birthday. The senate complied, and Marcus served under Antoninus, the consul for 139.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[58]</a></sup> Marcus\' adoption diverted him from the typical career path of his class. If not for his adoption, he probably would have become <i><a href="/wiki/Triumvir_Monetalis" title="Triumvir Monetalis">triumvir monetalis</a></i>, a highly regarded post involving token administration of the state mint; after that, he could have served as <a href="/wiki/Military_tribune" title="Military tribune">tribune with a legion</a>, becoming the legion\'s nominal second-in-command. Marcus probably would have opted for travel and further education instead. As it was, Marcus was set apart from his fellow citizens. Nonetheless, his biographer attests that his character remained unaffected: \'He still showed the same respect to his relations as he had when he was an ordinary citizen, and he was as thrifty and careful of his possessions as he had been when he lived in a private household\'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[59]</a></sup>\n</p><p>After a series of suicide attempts, all thwarted by Antoninus, Hadrian left for <a href="/wiki/Baiae" title="Baiae">Baiae</a>, a seaside resort on the <a href="/wiki/Campania" title="Campania">Campanian</a> coast. His condition did not improve, and he abandoned the diet prescribed by his doctors, indulging himself in food and drink. He sent for Antoninus, who was at his side when he died on 10 July 138.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[60]</a></sup> His remains were buried quietly at <a href="/wiki/Pozzuoli" title="Pozzuoli">Puteoli</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[61]</a></sup> The succession to Antoninus was peaceful and stable: Antoninus kept Hadrian\'s nominees in office and appeased the senate, respecting its privileges and commuting the death sentences of men charged in Hadrian\'s last days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[62]</a></sup> For his dutiful behaviour, Antoninus was asked to accept the name \'Pius\'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[63]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Heir_to_Antoninus_Pius_.28138.E2.80.93145.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Heir_to_Antoninus_Pius_(138&#8211;145)">Heir to Antoninus Pius (138&#8211;145)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Marcus_Aurelius&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Heir to Antoninus Pius (138&#8211;145)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Antoninus_Pius,_sestertius,_AD_140-144,_RIC_III_601.jpg"><img alt="Coin commemorating the betrothal of Marcus Aurelius to his eventual wife Faustina" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="394" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Antoninus_Pius%2C_sestertius%2C_AD_140-144%2C_RIC_III_601.jpg/300px-Antoninus_Pius%2C_sestertius%2C_AD_140-144%2C_RIC_III_601.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Antoninus_Pius%2C_sestertius%2C_AD_140-144%2C_RIC_III_601.jpg/450px-Antoninus_Pius%2C_sestertius%2C_AD_140-144%2C_RIC_III_601.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Antoninus_Pius%2C_sestertius%2C_AD_140-144%2C_RIC_III_601.jpg/600px-Antoninus_Pius%2C_sestertius%2C_AD_140-144%2C_RIC_III_601.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Antoninus_Pius,_sestertius,_AD_140-144,_RIC_III_601.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Sestertius" title="Sestertius">Sestertius</a> of <a href="/wiki/Antoninus_Pius" title="Antoninus Pius">Antoninus Pius</a> (AD 140-144). It celebrates the betrothal of Marcus Aurelus and <a href="/wiki/Faustina_the_Younger" title="Faustina the Younger">Faustina the Younger</a> in 139, pictured below Antoninus, who is holding a statuette of <a href="/wiki/Concordia_(mythology)" title="Concordia (mythology)">Concordia</a> and clasping hands with <a href="/wiki/Faustina_the_Elder" title="Faustina the Elder">Faustina the Elder</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[64]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Antoninus_Pius,_with_Marcus_Aurelius_Caesar,_denarius,_AD_139,_RIC_III_412a.jpg"><img alt="Coin of Antoninus Pius, Marcus\' predecessor, depicting Antoninus on the obverse and Marcus on the reverse" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="389" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Antoninus_Pius%2C_with_Marcus_Aurelius_Caesar%2C_denarius%2C_AD_139%2C_RIC_III_412a.jpg/300px-Antoninus_Pius%2C_with_Marcus_Aurelius_Caesar%2C_denarius%2C_AD_139%2C_RIC_III_412a.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Antoninus_Pius%2C_with_Marcus_Aurelius_Caesar%2C_denarius%2C_AD_139%2C_RIC_III_412a.jpg/450px-Antoninus_Pius%2C_with_Marcus_Aurelius_Caesar%2C_denarius%2C_AD_139%2C_RIC_III_412a.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Antoninus_Pius%2C_with_Marcus_Aurelius_Caesar%2C_denarius%2C_AD_139%2C_RIC_III_412a.jpg/600px-Antoninus_Pius%2C_with_Marcus_Aurelius_Caesar%2C_denarius%2C_AD_139%2C_RIC_III_412a.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Antoninus_Pius,_with_Marcus_Aurelius_Caesar,_denarius,_AD_139,_RIC_III_412a.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Denarius" title="Denarius">Denarius</a> of Antoninus Pius (AD 139), with a portrait of Marcus Aurelius on the reverse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[65]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>Immediately after Hadrian\'s death, Antoninus approached Marcus and requested that his marriage arrangements be amended: Marcus\' betrothal to <a href="/wiki/Ceionia_Fabia" title="Ceionia Fabia">Ceionia Fabia</a> would be annulled, and he would be betrothed to <a href="/wiki/Faustina_the_Younger" title="Faustina the Younger">Faustina</a>, Antoninus\' daughter, instead. Faustina\'s betrothal to Ceionia\'s brother Lucius Commodus would also have to be annulled. Marcus consented to Antoninus\' proposal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[66]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Antoninus bolstered Marcus\' dignity: Marcus was made <a href="/wiki/Roman_consul" title="Roman consul">consul</a> for 140, with Antoninus as his colleague, and was appointed as a <i>seviri</i>, one of the <a href="/wiki/Equites" title="Equites">knights</a>\' six commanders, at the order\'s annual parade on 15 July 139. As the heir apparent, Marcus became <i>princeps iuventutis</i>, head of the equestrian order. He now took the name Caesar: Marcus Aelius Aurelius Verus Caesar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[67]</a></sup> Marcus would later caution himself against taking the name too seriously: \'See that you do not turn into a Caesar; do not be dipped into the <a href="/wiki/Tyrian_purple" title="Tyrian purple">purple dye</a>&#8212;for that can happen\'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[68]</a></sup> At the senate\'s request, Marcus joined all the priestly colleges (<i><a href="/wiki/Pontiff" title="Pontiff">pontifices</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Augur" title="Augur">augures</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Quindecimviri_sacris_faciundis" title="Quindecimviri sacris faciundis">quindecimviri sacris faciundis</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Epulones" title="Epulones">septemviri epulonum</a></i>, etc.);<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-73">[69]</a></sup> direct evidence for membership, however, is available only for the <a href="/wiki/Arval_Brethren" title="Arval Brethren">Arval Brethren</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[70]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Antoninus demanded that Marcus take up residence in the House of Tiberius, the imperial palace on the Palatine. Antoninus also made him take up the habits of his new station, the <i>aulicum fastigium</i> or \'pomp of the court\', against Marcus\' objections.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_73-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-73">[69]</a></sup> Marcus would struggle to reconcile the life of the court with his philosophic yearnings. He told himself it was an attainable goal&#8212;\'Where life is possible, then it is possible to live the right life; life is possible in a palace, so it is possible to live the right life in a palace\'<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[71]</a></sup>&#8212;but he found it difficult nonetheless. He would criticize himself in the <i>Meditations</i> for \'abusing court life\' in front of company.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[72]</a></sup>\n</p><p>As quaestor, Marcus would have had little real administrative work to do. He would read imperial letters to the senate when Antoninus was absent, and would do secretarial work for the senators. His duties as consul were more significant: one of two senior representatives of the senate, he would preside over meetings and take a major role in the body\'s administrative functions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[73]</a></sup> He felt drowned in paperwork, and complained to his tutor, Marcus Cornelius Fronto: \'I am so out of breath from dictating nearly thirty letters\'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[74]</a></sup> He was being \'fitted for ruling the state\', in the words of his biographer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[75]</a></sup> He was required to make a speech to the assembled senators as well, making oratorical training essential for the job.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._89_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._89-80">[76]</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 1 January 145, Marcus was made consul a second time. He might have been unwell at this time: a letter from Fronto that might have been sent at this time urges Marcus to have plenty of sleep \'so that you may come into the Senate with a good colour and read your speech with a strong voice\'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[77]</a></sup> Marcus had complained of an illness in an earlier letter: \'As far as my strength is concerned, I am beginning to get it back; and there is no trace of the pain in my chest. But that ulcer [...]<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[notes 5]</a></sup> I am having treatment and taking care not to do anything that interferes with it\'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[78]</a></sup> Marcus was never particularly healthy or strong. Cassius Dio, writing of his later years, praised him for behaving dutifully in spite of his various illnesses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[79]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In April 145, Marcus married Faustina, as had been planned since 138. Since Marcus was, by adoption, Antoninus\' son, under Roman law he was marrying his sister; Antoninus would have had to formally release one or the other from his paternal authority (his <i>patria potestas</i>) for the ceremony to take place.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[80]</a></sup> Little is specifically known of the ceremony, but it is said to have been \'noteworthy\'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[81]</a></sup> Coins were issued with the heads of the couple, and Antoninus, as <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pontifex_Maximus" title="Pontifex Maximus">Pontifex Maximus</a></i>, would have officiated. Marcus makes no apparent reference to the marriage in his surviving letters, and only sparing references to Faustina.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[82]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fronto_and_further_education">Fronto and further education</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Marcus_Aurelius&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Fronto and further education">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>After taking the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Toga_virilis" title="Toga virilis">toga virilis</a></i> in 136, Marcus probably began his training in <a href="/wiki/Eloquence" title="Eloquence">oratory</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[83]</a></sup> He had three tutors in <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> &#8211; Aninus Macer, Caninius Celer, and <a href="/wiki/Herodes_Atticus" title="Herodes Atticus">Herodes Atticus</a> &#8211; and one in Latin &#8211; Fronto. The latter two were the most esteemed orators of the day,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[84]</a></sup> but probably did not become his tutors until his adoption by Antoninus in 138. The preponderance of Greek tutors indicates the importance of the <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek language</a> to the aristocracy of Rome.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[85]</a></sup> This was the age of the <a href="/wiki/Second_Sophistic" title="Second Sophistic">Second Sophistic</a>, a renaissance in Greek letters. Although educated in Rome, in his <i>Meditations</i>, Marcus would write his inmost thoughts in Greek.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[86]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Atticus was controversial: an enormously rich Athenian (probably the richest man in the eastern half of the empire), he was quick to anger, and resented by his fellow Athenians for his patronizing manner.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[87]</a></sup> Atticus was an inveterate opponent of <a href="/wiki/Stoicism" title="Stoicism">Stoicism</a> and philosophic pretensions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[88]</a></sup> He thought the Stoics\' desire for a \'lack of feeling\' foolish: they would live a \'sluggish, enervated life\', he said.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[89]</a></sup> Marcus would become a Stoic. He would not mention Herodes at all in his <i>Meditations</i>, in spite of the fact that they would come into contact many times over the following decades.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[90]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Fronto was highly esteemed: in the self-consciously antiquarian world of Latin letters,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[91]</a></sup> he was thought of as second only to <a href="/wiki/Cicero" title="Cicero">Cicero</a>, perhaps even an alternative to him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-97">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[notes 6]</a></sup> He did not care much for Atticus, though Marcus was eventually to put the pair on speaking terms. Fronto exercised a complete mastery of Latin, capable of tracing expressions through the literature, producing obscure <a href="/wiki/Synonym" title="Synonym">synonyms</a>, and challenging minor improprieties in word choice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_97-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-97">[92]</a></sup>\n</p><p>A significant amount of the correspondence between Fronto and Marcus has survived.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._69_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._69-102">[96]</a></sup> The pair were very close. \'Farewell my Fronto, wherever you are, my most sweet love and delight. How is it between you and me? I love you and you are not here\'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[97]</a></sup> Marcus spent time with Fronto\'s wife and daughter, both named Cratia, and they enjoyed light conversation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[98]</a></sup>\n</p><p>He wrote Fronto a letter on his birthday, claiming to love him as he loved himself, and calling on the gods to ensure that every word he learned of literature, he would learn \'from the lips of Fronto\'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[99]</a></sup> His prayers for Fronto\'s health were more than conventional, because Fronto was frequently ill; at times, he seems to be an almost constant invalid, always suffering<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[100]</a></sup> &#8212; about one-quarter of the surviving letters deal with the man\'s sicknesses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[101]</a></sup> Marcus asks that Fronto\'s pain be inflicted on himself, \'of my own accord with every kind of discomfort\'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[102]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Fronto never became Marcus\' full-time teacher, and continued his career as an advocate. One notorious case brought him into conflict with Atticus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[103]</a></sup> Marcus pleaded with Fronto, first with \'advice\', then as a \'favour\', not to attack Atticus; he had already asked Atticus to refrain from making the first blows.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[104]</a></sup> Fronto replied that he was surprised to discover Marcus counted Atticus as a friend (perhaps Atticus was not yet Marcus\' tutor), and allowed that Marcus might be correct,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[105]</a></sup> but nonetheless affirmed his intent to win the case by any means necessary: \'[T]he charges are frightful and must be spoken of as frightful. Those in particular which refer to the beating and robbing I will describe in such a way that they savour of gall and bile. If I happen to call him an uneducated little Greek it will not mean war to the death\'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[106]</a></sup> The outcome of the trial is unknown.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[107]</a></sup>\n</p><p>By the age of twenty-five (between April 146 and April 147), Marcus had grown disaffected with his studies in <a href="/wiki/Jurisprudence" title="Jurisprudence">jurisprudence</a>, and showed some signs of general <a href="/wiki/Malaise" title="Malaise">malaise</a>. His master, he writes to Fronto, was an unpleasant blowhard, and had made \'a hit at\' him: \'It is easy to sit yawning next to a judge, he says, but to <i>be</i> a judge is noble work\'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[108]</a></sup> Marcus had grown tired of his exercises, of taking positions in imaginary debates. When he criticized the insincerity of conventional language, Fronto took to defend it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[109]</a></sup> In any case, Marcus\' formal education was now over. He had kept his teachers on good terms, following them devotedly. It \'affected his health adversely\', his biographer writes, to have devoted so much effort to his studies. It was the only thing the biographer could find fault with in Marcus\' entire boyhood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[110]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Fronto had warned Marcus against the study of philosophy early on: \'It is better never to have touched the teaching of philosophy...than to have tasted it superficially, with the edge of the lips, as the saying is\'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[111]</a></sup> He disdained philosophy and philosophers, and looked down on Marcus\' sessions with <a href="/wiki/Apollonius_of_Chalcedon" title="Apollonius of Chalcedon">Apollonius of Chalcedon</a> and others in this circle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._69_102-1"><a href="#cite_note-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._69-102">[96]</a></sup> Fronto put an uncharitable interpretation of Marcus\' \'conversion to philosophy\': \'In the fashion of the young, tired of boring work\', Marcus had turned to philosophy to escape the constant exercises of oratorical training.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[112]</a></sup> Marcus kept in close touch with Fronto, but would ignore Fronto\'s scruples.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[113]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Apollonius may have introduced Marcus to Stoic philosophy, but <a href="/wiki/Junius_Rusticus" title="Junius Rusticus">Quintus Junius Rusticus</a> would have the strongest influence on the boy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[114]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[notes 7]</a></sup> He was the man Fronto recognized as having \'wooed Marcus away\' from oratory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[116]</a></sup> He was older than Fronto and twenty years older than Marcus. As the grandson of <a href="/wiki/Arulenus_Rusticus" title="Arulenus Rusticus">Arulenus Rusticus</a>, one of the martyrs to the tyranny of <a href="/wiki/Domitian" title="Domitian">Domitian</a> (<i>r</i>. 81&#8211;96), he was heir to the tradition of \'<a href="/wiki/Stoic_Opposition" title="Stoic Opposition">Stoic Opposition</a>\' to the \'bad emperors\' of the 1st century;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[117]</a></sup> the true successor of <a href="/wiki/Seneca_the_Younger" title="Seneca the Younger">Seneca</a> (as opposed to Fronto, the false one).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[118]</a></sup> Marcus thanks Rusticus for teaching him \'not to be led astray into enthusiasm for rhetoric, for writing on speculative themes, for discoursing on moralizing texts.... To avoid oratory, poetry, and \'fine writing<i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[119]</a></sup></i>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Births_and_deaths">Births and deaths</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Marcus_Aurelius&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Births and deaths">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>On November 30, 147, Faustina gave birth to a girl named Domitia Faustina. She was the first of at least thirteen children (including two sets of twins) that Faustina would bear over the next twenty-three years. The next day, 1 December, Antoninus gave Marcus the <a href="/wiki/Tribune" title="Tribune">tribunician</a> power and the <i><a href="/wiki/Imperium" title="Imperium">imperium</a></i> &#8212; authority over the armies and provinces of the emperor. As tribune, he had the right to bring one measure before the senate after the four Antoninus could introduce. His tribunican powers would be renewed with Antoninus\' on 10 December 147.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._103_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._103-127">[120]</a></sup> The first mention of Domitia in Marcus\' letters reveals her as a sickly infant. \'Caesar to Fronto. If the gods are willing we seem to have a hope of recovery. The diarrhea has stopped, the little attacks of fever have been driven away. But the emaciation is still extreme and there is still quite a bit of coughing\'. He and Faustina, Marcus wrote, had been \'pretty occupied\' with the girl\'s care.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[121]</a></sup> Domitia would die in 151.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[122]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:RomaCastelSantAngelo-2.jpg"><img alt="Mausoleum of Hadrian" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="945" data-file-width="1491" decoding="async" height="190" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/RomaCastelSantAngelo-2.jpg/300px-RomaCastelSantAngelo-2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/RomaCastelSantAngelo-2.jpg/450px-RomaCastelSantAngelo-2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/RomaCastelSantAngelo-2.jpg/600px-RomaCastelSantAngelo-2.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:RomaCastelSantAngelo-2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mausoleum_of_Hadrian" title="Mausoleum of Hadrian">Mausoleum of Hadrian</a>, where the children of Marcus and Faustina were buried</div></div></div><p>\nIn 149, Faustina gave birth again, to twin sons. Contemporary coinage commemorates the event, with crossed cornucopiae beneath portrait busts of the two small boys, and the legend <i>temporum felicitas</i>, \'the happiness of the times\'. They did not survive long. Before the end of the year, another family coin was issued: it shows only a tiny girl, Domitia Faustina, and one boy baby. Then another: the girl alone. The infants were buried in the <a href="/wiki/Castel_Sant%27Angelo" title="Castel Sant\'Angelo">Mausoleum of Hadrian</a>, where their epitaphs survive. They were called Titus Aurelius Antoninus and Tiberius Aelius Aurelius.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_pp._206&#8211;07_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_pp._206&#8211;07-130">[123]</a></sup> Marcus steadied himself: \'One man prays: \'How I may not lose my little child\', but you must pray: \'How I may not be afraid to lose him<i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[124]</a></sup> He quoted from the </i><a href="/wiki/Iliad" title="Iliad">Iliad</a><i> what he called the \'briefest and most familiar saying...enough to dispel sorrow and fear\':</i></p><i></i><blockquote><p><i><div class="poem">\n<p>&#160;leaves,<br/>\nthe wind scatters some on the face of the ground;<br/>\nlike unto them are the children of men.\n</p>\n</div></i>\n&#8211;&#160;<i>Iliad</i> vi.146<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[125]</a></sup></p></blockquote>\n<p>Another daughter was born on 7 March 150, <a href="/wiki/Lucilla" title="Lucilla">Annia Aurelia Galeria Lucilla</a>. At some time between 155 and 161, probably soon after 155, Marcus\' mother Domitia Lucilla died.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_107_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_107-133">[126]</a></sup> Faustina probably had another daughter in 151, but the child, <a href="/wiki/Annia_Galeria_Aurelia_Faustina" title="Annia Galeria Aurelia Faustina">Annia Galeria Aurelia Faustina</a>, might not have been born until 153.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[127]</a></sup> Another son, Tiberius Aelius Antoninus, was born in 152. A coin issue celebrates <i>fecunditati Augustae</i>, \'the Augusta\'s fertility\', depicting two girls and an infant. The boy did not survive long; on coins from 156, only the two girls were depicted. He might have died in 152, the same year as Marcus\' sister Cornificia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._108_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._108-135">[128]</a></sup> By 28 March 158, when Marcus replied, another of his children was dead. Marcus thanked the temple synod, \'even though this turned out otherwise\'. The child\'s name is unknown.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[129]</a></sup> In 159 and 160, Faustina gave birth to daughters: Fadilla and Cornificia, named respectively after Faustina\'s and Marcus\' dead sisters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._114_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._114-137">[130]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Antoninus_Pius.27_last_years"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Antoninus_Pius\'_last_years">Antoninus Pius\' last years</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Marcus_Aurelius&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Antoninus Pius\' last years">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Imperator_Antoninus_Pius.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3066" data-file-width="2300" decoding="async" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Imperator_Antoninus_Pius.jpg/220px-Imperator_Antoninus_Pius.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Imperator_Antoninus_Pius.jpg/330px-Imperator_Antoninus_Pius.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Imperator_Antoninus_Pius.jpg/440px-Imperator_Antoninus_Pius.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Imperator_Antoninus_Pius.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bust of <a href="/wiki/Antoninus_Pius" title="Antoninus Pius">Antoninus Pius</a>, <a href="/wiki/British_Museum" title="British Museum">British Museum</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Lucius started his political career as a quaestor in 153. He was consul in 154,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[131]</a></sup> and was consul again with Marcus in 161.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-livius1_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-livius1-139">[132]</a></sup> Lucius had no other titles, except that of \'son of Augustus\'. Lucius had a markedly different personality from Marcus: he enjoyed sports of all kinds, but especially hunting and wrestling; he took obvious pleasure in the circus games and gladiatorial fights.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[133]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[notes 8]</a></sup> He did not marry until 164.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[137]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 156, Antoninus turned 70. He found it difficult to keep himself upright without <a href="/wiki/Corset" title="Corset">stays</a>. He started nibbling on dry bread to give him the strength to stay awake through his morning receptions. As Antoninus aged, Marcus would take on more administrative duties, more still when he became the <a href="/wiki/Praetorian_prefect" title="Praetorian prefect">praetorian prefect</a> (an office that was as much secretarial as military) as Gavius Maximus died in 156 or 157.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[138]</a></sup> In 160, Marcus and Lucius were designated joint consuls for the following year. Antoninus may have already been ill.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._114_137-1"><a href="#cite_note-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._114-137">[130]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Two days before his death, the biographer reports, Antoninus was at his ancestral estate at <a href="/wiki/Lorium" title="Lorium">Lorium</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Etruria" title="Etruria">Etruria</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[139]</a></sup> about 19 kilometres (12&#160;mi) from Rome.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Victor,_15:7_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-Victor,_15:7-148">[140]</a></sup> He ate Alpine cheese at dinner quite greedily. In the night he vomited; he had a fever the next day. The day after that, 7 March 161,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[141]</a></sup> he summoned the imperial council, and passed the state and his daughter to Marcus. The emperor gave the keynote to his life in the last word that he uttered when the <a href="/wiki/Tribune" title="Tribune">tribune</a> of the night-watch came to ask the password&#8212;\'aequanimitas\' (equanimity).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bury_532_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bury_532-150">[142]</a></sup> He then turned over, as if going to sleep, and died.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[143]</a></sup> His death closed out the longest reign since Augustus, surpassing <a href="/wiki/Tiberius" title="Tiberius">Tiberius</a> by a couple of months.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[144]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Emperor">Emperor</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Marcus_Aurelius&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Emperor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Reign_of_Marcus_Aurelius" title="Reign of Marcus Aurelius">Reign of Marcus Aurelius</a></div>\n<h3><span id="Accession_of_Marcus_Aurelius_and_Lucius_Verus_.28161.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Accession_of_Marcus_Aurelius_and_Lucius_Verus_(161)">Accession of Marcus Aurelius and Lucius Verus (161)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Marcus_Aurelius&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Accession of Marcus Aurelius and Lucius Verus (161)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Co-emperors_Marcus_Aurelius_and_Lucius_Verus,_British_Museum_(23455313842).jpg"><img alt="Busts of Marcus Aurelius and his co-ruler Lucius Verus" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4613" data-file-width="3190" decoding="async" height="318" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Co-emperors_Marcus_Aurelius_and_Lucius_Verus%2C_British_Museum_%2823455313842%29.jpg/220px-Co-emperors_Marcus_Aurelius_and_Lucius_Verus%2C_British_Museum_%2823455313842%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Co-emperors_Marcus_Aurelius_and_Lucius_Verus%2C_British_Museum_%2823455313842%29.jpg/330px-Co-emperors_Marcus_Aurelius_and_Lucius_Verus%2C_British_Museum_%2823455313842%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Co-emperors_Marcus_Aurelius_and_Lucius_Verus%2C_British_Museum_%2823455313842%29.jpg/440px-Co-emperors_Marcus_Aurelius_and_Lucius_Verus%2C_British_Museum_%2823455313842%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Co-emperors_Marcus_Aurelius_and_Lucius_Verus,_British_Museum_(23455313842).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Busts of the co-emperors Marcus Aurelius (left) and <a href="/wiki/Lucius_Verus" title="Lucius Verus">Lucius Verus</a> (right), <a href="/wiki/British_Museum" title="British Museum">British Museum</a></div></div></div>\n<p>After Antoninus died in 161, Marcus was effectively sole ruler of the Empire. The formalities of the position would follow. The senate would soon grant him the name Augustus and the title <i><a href="/wiki/Imperator" title="Imperator">imperator</a></i>, and he would soon be formally elected as <i>Pontifex Maximus</i>, chief priest of the official cults. Marcus made some show of resistance: the biographer writes that he was \'compelled\' to take imperial power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[145]</a></sup> This may have been a genuine <i>horror imperii</i>, \'fear of imperial power\'. Marcus, with his preference for the philosophic life, found the imperial office unappealing. His training as a Stoic, however, had made the choice clear that it was his duty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[146]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Although Marcus showed no personal affection for Hadrian (significantly, he does not thank him in the first book of his <i>Meditations</i>), he presumably believed it his duty to enact the man\'s succession plans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[147]</a></sup> Thus, although the senate planned to confirm Marcus alone, he refused to take office unless Lucius received equal powers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[148]</a></sup> The senate accepted, granting Lucius the <i>imperium</i>, the tribunician power, and the name Augustus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[149]</a></sup> Marcus became, in official titulature, Imperator Caesar Marcus Aurelius Antoninus Augustus; Lucius, forgoing his name Commodus and taking Marcus\' family name Verus, became Imperator Caesar Lucius Aurelius Verus Augustus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[150]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[notes 9]</a></sup> It was the first time that Rome was ruled by two emperors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._117_161-1"><a href="#cite_note-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._117-161">[153]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[notes 10]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In spite of their nominal equality, Marcus held more <i><a href="/wiki/Auctoritas" title="Auctoritas">auctoritas</a></i>, or \'authority\', than Lucius. He had been consul once more than Lucius, he had shared in Antoninus\' rule, and he alone was <i>Pontifex Maximus</i>. It would have been clear to the public which emperor was the more senior.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._117_161-3"><a href="#cite_note-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._117-161">[153]</a></sup> As the biographer wrote, \'Verus obeyed Marcus...as a lieutenant obeys a proconsul or a governor obeys the emperor\'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[154]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Immediately after their senate confirmation, the emperors proceeded to the <a href="/wiki/Castra_Praetoria" title="Castra Praetoria">Castra Praetoria</a>, the camp of the <a href="/wiki/Praetorian_Guard" title="Praetorian Guard">Praetorian Guard</a>. Lucius addressed the assembled troops, which then acclaimed the pair as <i>imperatores</i>. Then, like every new emperor since <a href="/wiki/Claudius" title="Claudius">Claudius</a>, Lucius promised the troops a special donative.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[155]</a></sup> This donative, however, was twice the size of those past: 20,000 <a href="/wiki/Sestertius" title="Sestertius">sesterces</a> (5,000 <a href="/wiki/Denarius" title="Denarius">denarii</a>) per capita, with more to officers. In return for this bounty, equivalent to several years\' pay, the troops swore an oath to protect the emperors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[156]</a></sup> The ceremony was perhaps not entirely necessary, given that Marcus\' accession had been peaceful and unopposed, but it was good insurance against later military troubles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._118_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._118-167">[157]</a></sup> Upon his accession he also devalued the <a href="/wiki/Roman_currency" title="Roman currency">Roman currency</a>. He decreased the silver purity of the denarius from 83.5% to 79% &#8212; the silver weight dropping from 2.68 grams to 2.57 grams.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TulaneUniversity_168-0"><a href="#cite_note-TulaneUniversity-168">[158]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Antoninus\' funeral ceremonies were, in the words of the biographer, \'elaborate\'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[159]</a></sup> If his funeral followed those of his predecessors, his body would have been incinerated on a pyre at the <a href="/wiki/Campus_Martius" title="Campus Martius">Campus Martius</a>, and his spirit would have been seen as ascending to the gods\' home in the heavens. Marcus and Lucius nominated their father for deification. In contrast to their behaviour during Antoninus\' campaign to deify Hadrian, the senate did not oppose the emperors\' wishes. A <i><a href="/wiki/Flamen" title="Flamen">flamen</a></i>, or cultic priest, was appointed to minister the cult of the deified Divus Antoninus. Antoninus\' remains were laid to rest in Hadrian\'s mausoleum, beside the remains of Marcus\' children and of Hadrian himself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[160]</a></sup> The temple he had dedicated to his wife, Diva Faustina, became the <a href="/wiki/Temple_of_Antoninus_and_Faustina" title="Temple of Antoninus and Faustina">Temple of Antoninus and Faustina</a>. It survives as the church of San Lorenzo in Miranda.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._118_167-1"><a href="#cite_note-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._118-167">[157]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In accordance with his will, Antoninus\' fortune passed on to Faustina.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[161]</a></sup> (Marcus had little need of his wife\'s fortune. Indeed, at his accession, Marcus transferred part of his mother\'s estate to his nephew, Ummius Quadratus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[162]</a></sup>) Faustina was three months pregnant at her husband\'s accession. During the pregnancy she dreamed of giving birth to two serpents, one fiercer than the other.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[163]</a></sup> On 31 August she gave birth at <a href="/wiki/Lanuvium" title="Lanuvium">Lanuvium</a> to twins: T. Aurelius Fulvus Antoninus and Lucius Aurelius Commodus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[164]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[notes 11]</a></sup> Aside from the fact that the twins shared <a href="/wiki/Caligula" title="Caligula">Caligula</a>\'s birthday, the omens were favorable, and the astrologers drew positive horoscopes for the children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[166]</a></sup> The births were celebrated on the imperial coinage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[167]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_rule">Early rule</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Marcus_Aurelius&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Early rule">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Soon after the emperors\' accession, Marcus\' eleven-year-old daughter, Annia Lucilla, was betrothed to Lucius (in spite of the fact that he was, formally, her uncle).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[168]</a></sup> At the ceremonies commemorating the event, new provisions were made for the support of poor children, along the lines of earlier imperial foundations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[169]</a></sup> Marcus and Lucius proved popular with the people of Rome, who strongly approved of their <i>civiliter</i> (\'lacking pomp\') behaviour. The emperors permitted free speech, evidenced by the fact that the comedy writer Marullus was able to criticize them without suffering retribution. As the biographer wrote, \'No one missed the lenient ways of Pius\'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[170]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Marcus replaced a number of the empire\'s major officials. The <i><a href="/wiki/Ab_epistulis" title="Ab epistulis">ab epistulis</a></i> Sextus Caecilius Crescens Volusianus, in charge of the imperial correspondence, was replaced with Titus Varius Clemens. Clemens was from the frontier province of <a href="/wiki/Pannonia" title="Pannonia">Pannonia</a> and had served in the war in <a href="/wiki/Mauretania" title="Mauretania">Mauretania</a>. Recently, he had served as procurator of five provinces. He was a man suited for a time of military crisis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[171]</a></sup> Lucius Volusius Maecianus, Marcus\' former tutor, had been <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Augustal_prefect" title="Augustal prefect">prefectural governor</a> of <a href="/wiki/Egypt_(Roman_province)" title="Egypt (Roman province)">Egypt</a> at Marcus\' accession. Maecianus was recalled, made senator, and appointed prefect of the treasury (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aerarium_Saturni" title="Aerarium Saturni">aerarium Saturni</a></i>). He was made consul soon after.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[172]</a></sup> Fronto\'s son-in-law, <a href="/wiki/Gaius_Aufidius_Victorinus" title="Gaius Aufidius Victorinus">Gaius Aufidius Victorinus</a>, was appointed governor of <a href="/wiki/Germania_Superior" title="Germania Superior">Germania Superior</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HA_Marcus_8_1985_184-0"><a href="#cite_note-HA_Marcus_8_1985-184">[173]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Fronto returned to his Roman townhouse at dawn on 28 March, having left his home in <a href="/wiki/Cirta" title="Cirta">Cirta</a> as soon as news of his pupils\' accession reached him. He sent a note to the imperial freedman Charilas, asking if he could call on the emperors. Fronto would later explain that he had not dared to write the emperors directly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[174]</a></sup> The tutor was immensely proud of his students. Reflecting on the speech he had written on taking his consulship in 143, when he had praised the young Marcus, Fronto was ebullient: \'There was then an outstanding natural ability in you; there is now perfected excellence. There was then a crop of growing corn; there is now a ripe, gathered harvest. What I was hoping for then, I have now. The hope has become a reality.\'<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[175]</a></sup> Fronto called on Marcus alone; neither thought to invite Lucius.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._120_187-0"><a href="#cite_note-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._120-187">[176]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Lucius was less esteemed by Fronto than his brother, as his interests were on a lower level. Lucius asked Fronto to adjudicate in a dispute he and his friend Calpurnius were having on the relative merits of two actors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[177]</a></sup> Marcus told Fronto of his reading &#8212; <a href="/wiki/Lucius_Coelius_Antipater" title="Lucius Coelius Antipater">Coelius</a> and a little Cicero &#8212; and his family. His daughters were in Rome with their great-great-aunt Matidia; Marcus thought the evening air of the country was too cold for them. He asked Fronto for \'some particularly eloquent reading matter, something of your own, or Cato, or Cicero, or Sallust or Gracchus&#8212;or some poet, for I need distraction, especially in this kind of way, by reading something that will uplift and diffuse my pressing anxieties.\'<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[178]</a></sup> Marcus\' early reign proceeded smoothly; he was able to give himself wholly to philosophy and the pursuit of popular affection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190"><a href="#cite_note-190">[179]</a></sup> Soon, however, he would find he had many anxieties. It would mean the end of the <i>felicitas temporum</i> (\'happy times\') that the coinage of 161 had proclaimed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[180]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303569">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti>.thumbinner{width:100%!important;max-width:none!important}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:none!important;width:100%!important;text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:492px;max-width:492px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:244px;max-width:244px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:181px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:12_dicembre_2008_piena_del_tevere_17.JPG"><img alt="Tiber Island in flood, December 2008" data-file-height="1944" //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/12_dicembre_2008_piena_del_tevere_17.JPG/484px-12_dicembre_2008_piena_del_tevere_17.JPG 2x" width="242"/></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:244px;max-width:244px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:181px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Piena_del_Tevere_-_Tiber_in_flood_-_Ponte_Sisto_-_Rome,_Italy_-_12_Dec._2008.jpg"><img alt="Tiber Island in flood, December 2008" data-file-height="2112" data-file-width="2816" decoding="async" height="182"
