1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Piena_del_Tevere_-_Tiber_in_flood_-_Ponte_Sisto_-_Rome%2C_Italy_-_12_Dec._2008.jpg/484px-Piena_del_Tevere_-_Tiber_in_flood_-_Ponte_Sisto_-_Rome%2C_Italy_-_12_Dec._2008.jpg 2x" width="242"/></a></div></div><div style="clear:left"></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent"><a href="/wiki/Tiber_Island" title="Tiber Island">Tiber Island</a> seen at a forty-year high-water mark of the <a href="/wiki/Tiber" title="Tiber">Tiber</a>, December 2008</div></div></div>\n<p>In either autumn 161 or spring 162,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193"><a href="#cite_note-193">[notes 12]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Tiber" title="Tiber">Tiber</a> overflowed its banks, flooding much of Rome. It drowned many animals, leaving the city in famine. Marcus and Lucius gave the crisis their personal attention.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[182]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[notes 13]</a></sup> In other times of famine, the emperors are said to have provided for the Italian communities out of the Roman granaries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197"><a href="#cite_note-197">[184]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Fronto\'s letters continued through Marcus\' early reign. Fronto felt that, because of Marcus\' prominence and public duties, lessons were more important now than they had ever been before. He believed Marcus was \'beginning to feel the wish to be eloquent once more, in spite of having for a time lost interest in eloquence\'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198"><a href="#cite_note-198">[185]</a></sup> Fronto would again remind his pupil of the tension between his role and his philosophic pretensions: \'Suppose, Caesar, that you can attain to the wisdom of <a href="/wiki/Cleanthes" title="Cleanthes">Cleanthes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zeno_of_Citium" title="Zeno of Citium">Zeno</a>, yet, against your will, not the philosopher\'s woolen cape\'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[186]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The early days of Marcus\' reign were the happiest of Fronto\'s life: Marcus was beloved by the people of Rome, an excellent emperor, a fond pupil, and perhaps most importantly, as eloquent as could be wished.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200"><a href="#cite_note-200">[187]</a></sup> Marcus had displayed rhetorical skill in his speech to the senate after an earthquake at <a href="/wiki/Cyzicus" title="Cyzicus">Cyzicus</a>. It had conveyed the drama of the disaster, and the senate had been awed: \'Not more suddenly or violently was the city stirred by the earthquake than the minds of your hearers by your speech\'. Fronto was hugely pleased.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201"><a href="#cite_note-201">[188]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="War_with_Parthia_.28161.E2.80.93166.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="War_with_Parthia_(161&#8211;166)">War with Parthia (161&#8211;166)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Marcus_Aurelius&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: War with Parthia (161&#8211;166)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Roman%E2%80%93Parthian_War_of_161%E2%80%93166" title="Roman&#8211;Parthian War of 161&#8211;166">Roman&#8211;Parthian War of 161&#8211;166</a></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Roman%E2%80%93Persian_Wars" title="Roman&#8211;Persian Wars">Roman&#8211;Persian Wars</a></div>\n<p>On his deathbed, Antoninus spoke of nothing but the state and the foreign kings who had wronged him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-202"><a href="#cite_note-202">[189]</a></sup> One of those kings, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vologases_IV_of_Parthia" title="Vologases IV of Parthia">Vologases IV of Parthia</a>, made his move in late summer or early autumn 161.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203"><a href="#cite_note-203">[190]</a></sup> Vologases entered the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Armenia_(antiquity)" title="Kingdom of Armenia (antiquity)">Kingdom of Armenia</a> (then a Roman client state), expelled its king and installed his own &#8212; <a href="/wiki/Bakur" title="Bakur">Pacorus</a>, an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arsacid_Empire" title="Arsacid Empire">Arsacid</a> like himself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-204"><a href="#cite_note-204">[191]</a></sup> The governor of Cappadocia, the frontline in all Armenian conflicts, was <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Sedatius_Severianus" title="Marcus Sedatius Severianus">Marcus Sedatius Severianus</a>, a Gaul with much experience in military matters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-205"><a href="#cite_note-205">[192]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Convinced by the prophet <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alexander_of_Abonutichus" title="Alexander of Abonutichus">Alexander of Abonutichus</a> that he could defeat the Parthians easily and win glory for himself,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206"><a href="#cite_note-206">[193]</a></sup> Severianus led a legion (perhaps the <a href="/wiki/Legio_IX_Hispana" title="Legio IX Hispana">IX Hispana</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-207"><a href="#cite_note-207">[194]</a></sup>) into Armenia, but was trapped by the great Parthian general Chosrhoes at Elegia, a town just beyond the Cappadocian frontiers, high up past the headwaters of the Euphrates. After Severianus made some unsuccessful efforts to engage Chosrhoes, he committed suicide, and his legion was massacred. The campaign had lasted only three days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-208"><a href="#cite_note-208">[195]</a></sup>\n</p><p>There was threat of war on other frontiers as well &#8212; in Britain, and in <a href="/wiki/Raetia" title="Raetia">Raetia</a> and Upper Germany, where the <a href="/wiki/Chatti" title="Chatti">Chatti</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Taunus" title="Taunus">Taunus</a> mountains had recently crossed over the <i><a href="/wiki/Limes" title="Limes">limes</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-209"><a href="#cite_note-209">[196]</a></sup> Marcus was unprepared. Antoninus seems to have given him no military experience; the biographer writes that Marcus spent the whole of Antoninus\' twenty-three-year reign at his emperor\'s side and not in the provinces, where most previous emperors had spent their early careers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-210"><a href="#cite_note-210">[197]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-212"><a href="#cite_note-212">[notes 14]</a></sup>\n</p><p>More bad news arrived: the Syrian governor\'s army had been defeated by the Parthians, and retreated in disarray.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-213"><a href="#cite_note-213">[199]</a></sup> Reinforcements were dispatched for the Parthian frontier. P. Julius Geminius Marcianus, an African senator commanding <a href="/wiki/Legio_X_Gemina" title="Legio X Gemina">X Gemina</a> at <a href="/wiki/Vindobona" title="Vindobona">Vindobona</a> (<a href="/wiki/Vienna" title="Vienna">Vienna</a>), left for Cappadocia with detachments from the Danubian legions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-214"><a href="#cite_note-214">[200]</a></sup> Three full legions were also sent east: <a href="/wiki/Legio_I_Minervia" title="Legio I Minervia">I Minervia</a> from Bonn in Upper Germany,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-215"><a href="#cite_note-215">[201]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Legio_II_Adiutrix" title="Legio II Adiutrix">II Adiutrix</a> from Aquincum,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-216"><a href="#cite_note-216">[202]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Legio_V_Macedonica" title="Legio V Macedonica">V Macedonica</a> from Troesmis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-217"><a href="#cite_note-217">[203]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The northern frontiers were strategically weakened; frontier governors were told to avoid conflict wherever possible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-218"><a href="#cite_note-218">[204]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Annius_Libo_(consul_161)" title="Marcus Annius Libo (consul 161)">M. Annius Libo</a>, Marcus\' first cousin, was sent to replace the Syrian governor. His first consulship was in 161, so he was probably in his early thirties,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-219"><a href="#cite_note-219">[205]</a></sup> and as a patrician, he lacked military experience. Marcus had chosen a reliable man rather than a talented one.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-220"><a href="#cite_note-220">[206]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Marcus_Aurelius,_aureus,_AD_166,_RIC_III_160.jpg"><img alt="Coin of Marcus Aurelius. Victoria appears on the reverse, commemorating Marcus\' Parthian victory." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="380" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Marcus_Aurelius%2C_aureus%2C_AD_166%2C_RIC_III_160.jpg/300px-Marcus_Aurelius%2C_aureus%2C_AD_166%2C_RIC_III_160.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Marcus_Aurelius%2C_aureus%2C_AD_166%2C_RIC_III_160.jpg/450px-Marcus_Aurelius%2C_aureus%2C_AD_166%2C_RIC_III_160.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Marcus_Aurelius%2C_aureus%2C_AD_166%2C_RIC_III_160.jpg/600px-Marcus_Aurelius%2C_aureus%2C_AD_166%2C_RIC_III_160.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Marcus_Aurelius,_aureus,_AD_166,_RIC_III_160.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Aureus of Marcus Aurelius (AD 166). On the reverse, <a href="/wiki/Victoria_(mythology)" title="Victoria (mythology)">Victoria</a> is holding a shield inscribed \'Vic(toria) Par(thica)\', referring to his victory against the Parthians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-221"><a href="#cite_note-221">[207]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>Marcus took a four-day public holiday at <a href="/wiki/Alsium" title="Alsium">Alsium</a>, a resort town on the coast of Etruria. He was too anxious to relax. Writing to Fronto, he declared that he would not speak about his holiday.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-222"><a href="#cite_note-222">[208]</a></sup> Fronto replied: \'What? Do I not know that you went to Alsium with the intention of devoting yourself to games, joking, and complete leisure for four whole days?\'<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-223"><a href="#cite_note-223">[209]</a></sup> He encouraged Marcus to rest, calling on the example of his predecessors (Antoninus had enjoyed exercise in the <i><a href="/wiki/Palaestra" title="Palaestra">palaestra</a></i>, fishing, and comedy),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-224"><a href="#cite_note-224">[210]</a></sup> going so far as to write up a fable about the gods\' division of the day between morning and evening &#8212; Marcus had apparently been spending most of his evenings on judicial matters instead of at leisure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-225"><a href="#cite_note-225">[211]</a></sup> Marcus could not take Fronto\'s advice. \'I have duties hanging over me that can hardly be begged off\', he wrote back.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-226"><a href="#cite_note-226">[212]</a></sup> Marcus Aurelius put on Fronto\'s voice to chastise himself: <i>Much good has my advice done you\', you will say!\' He had rested, and would rest often, but \'this devotion to duty! Who knows better than you how demanding it is!\'<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-227"><a href="#cite_note-227">[213]</a></sup></i>\n</p>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:492px;max-width:492px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:224px;max-width:224px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:174px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Antioch_in_Syria_engraving_by_William_Miller_after_H_Warren.jpg"><img alt="Depiction of Antioch, Syria" data-file-height="3596" data-file-width="4582" decoding="async" height="174" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Antioch_in_Syria_engraving_by_William_Miller_after_H_Warren.jpg/222px-Antioch_in_Syria_engraving_by_William_Miller_after_H_Warren.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Antioch_in_Syria_engraving_by_William_Miller_after_H_Warren.jpg/333px-Antioch_in_Syria_engraving_by_William_Miller_after_H_Warren.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Antioch_in_Syria_engraving_by_William_Miller_after_H_Warren.jpg/444px-Antioch_in_Syria_engraving_by_William_Miller_after_H_Warren.jpg 2x" width="222"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">The dissolute Syrian army spent more time in <a href="/wiki/Antioch" title="Antioch">Antioch</a>\'s open-air taverns than with their units.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-228"><a href="#cite_note-228">[214]</a></sup> (Engraving by <a href="/wiki/William_Miller_(engraver)" title="William Miller (engraver)">William Miller</a> after a drawing by H. Warren from a sketch by Captain <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Byam_Martin" title="Thomas Byam Martin">Byam Martin</a>, R.N., 1866)</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:264px;max-width:264px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:174px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ArRaqqahEuphrates.jpg"><img alt="Euphrates River" data-file-height="840" data-file-width="1260" decoding="async" height="175" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/ArRaqqahEuphrates.jpg/262px-ArRaqqahEuphrates.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/ArRaqqahEuphrates.jpg/393px-ArRaqqahEuphrates.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/ArRaqqahEuphrates.jpg/524px-ArRaqqahEuphrates.jpg 2x" width="262"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">The Euphrates River near <a href="/wiki/Raqqa" title="Raqqa">Raqqa</a>, Syria</div></div><div style="clear:left"></div></div></div>\n<p>Fronto sent Marcus a selection of reading material,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-229"><a href="#cite_note-229">[215]</a></sup> and, to settle his unease over the course of the Parthian war, a long and considered letter, full of historical references. In modern editions of Fronto\'s works, it is labeled <i>De bello Parthico</i> (<i>On the Parthian War</i>). There had been reverses in Rome\'s past, Fronto writes,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-230"><a href="#cite_note-230">[216]</a></sup> but in the end, Romans had always prevailed over their enemies: \'Always and everywhere [Mars] has changed our troubles into successes and our terrors into triumphs\'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-231"><a href="#cite_note-231">[217]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Over the winter of 161&#8211;162, news that a rebellion was brewing in Syria arrived and it was decided that Lucius should direct the Parthian war in person. He was stronger and healthier than Marcus, the argument went, and thus more suited to military activity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-232"><a href="#cite_note-232">[218]</a></sup> Lucius\' biographer suggests ulterior motives: to restrain Lucius\' debaucheries, to make him thrifty, to reform his morals by the terror of war, and to realize that he was an emperor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-233"><a href="#cite_note-233">[219]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-235"><a href="#cite_note-235">[notes 15]</a></sup> Whatever the case, the senate gave its assent, and, in the summer of 162, Lucius left. Marcus would remain in Rome, as the city \'demanded the presence of an emperor\'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-236"><a href="#cite_note-236">[221]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Lucius spent most of the campaign in Antioch, though he wintered at <a href="/wiki/Laodicea_in_Syria" title="Laodicea in Syria">Laodicea</a> and summered at Daphne, a resort just outside Antioch.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-237"><a href="#cite_note-237">[222]</a></sup> Critics declaimed Lucius\' luxurious lifestyle,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-238"><a href="#cite_note-238">[223]</a></sup> saying that he had taken to gambling, would \'dice the whole night through\',<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-239"><a href="#cite_note-239">[224]</a></sup> and enjoyed the company of actors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-240"><a href="#cite_note-240">[225]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-242"><a href="#cite_note-242">[notes 16]</a></sup> Libo died early in the war; perhaps Lucius had murdered him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-243"><a href="#cite_note-243">[227]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Statue_of_Lucilla_detail.jpg"><img alt="Statue of Marcus\' daughter Lucilla" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2008" data-file-width="1824" decoding="async" height="242" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Statue_of_Lucilla_detail.jpg/220px-Statue_of_Lucilla_detail.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Statue_of_Lucilla_detail.jpg/330px-Statue_of_Lucilla_detail.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Statue_of_Lucilla_detail.jpg/440px-Statue_of_Lucilla_detail.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Statue_of_Lucilla_detail.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Marble statue of <a href="/wiki/Lucilla" title="Lucilla">Lucilla</a>, 150&#8211;200 AD, <a href="/wiki/Bardo_National_Museum" title="Bardo National Museum">Bardo National Museum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tunisia" title="Tunisia">Tunisia</a></div></div></div>\n<p>In the middle of the war, perhaps in autumn 163 or early 164, Lucius made a trip to <a href="/wiki/Ephesus" title="Ephesus">Ephesus</a> to be married to Marcus\' daughter Lucilla.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-244"><a href="#cite_note-244">[228]</a></sup> Marcus moved up the date; perhaps he had already heard of Lucius\' mistress Panthea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-245"><a href="#cite_note-245">[229]</a></sup> Lucilla\'s thirteenth birthday was in March 163; whatever the date of her marriage, she was not yet fifteen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-246"><a href="#cite_note-246">[230]</a></sup> Lucilla was accompanied by her mother Faustina and Lucius\' uncle (his father\'s half-brother) M. Vettulenus Civica Barbarus,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-247"><a href="#cite_note-247">[231]</a></sup> who was made <i><a href="/wiki/Comes" title="Comes">comes</a> Augusti</i>, \'companion of the emperors\'. Marcus may have wanted Civica to watch over Lucius, the job Libo had failed at.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-248"><a href="#cite_note-248">[232]</a></sup> Marcus may have planned to accompany them all the way to Smyrna (the biographer says he told the senate he would), but this did not happen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-249"><a href="#cite_note-249">[233]</a></sup> He only accompanied the group as far as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brundisium" title="Brundisium">Brundisium</a>, where they boarded a ship for the east.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-250"><a href="#cite_note-250">[234]</a></sup> He returned to Rome immediately thereafter, and sent out special instructions to his proconsuls not to give the group any official reception.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-251"><a href="#cite_note-251">[235]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Armenian capital <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Artashat_(ancient_city)" title="Artashat (ancient city)">Artaxata</a> was captured in 163.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-252"><a href="#cite_note-252">[236]</a></sup> At the end of the year, Lucius took the title <i>Armeniacus</i>, despite having never seen combat; Marcus declined to accept the title until the following year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-253"><a href="#cite_note-253">[237]</a></sup> When Lucius was hailed as <i>imperator</i> again, however, Marcus did not hesitate to take the <i>Imperator II</i> with him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-254"><a href="#cite_note-254">[238]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Occupied Armenia was reconstructed on Roman terms. In 164, a new capital, Kaine Polis (\'New City\'), replaced Artaxata.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-255"><a href="#cite_note-255">[239]</a></sup> A new king was installed: a Roman senator of consular rank and Arsacid descent, <a href="/wiki/Sohaemus_of_Armenia" title="Sohaemus of Armenia">Gaius Julius Sohaemus</a>. He may not even have been crowned in Armenia; the ceremony may have taken place in Antioch, or even Ephesus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-256"><a href="#cite_note-256">[240]</a></sup> Sohaemus was hailed on the imperial coinage of 164 under the legend <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r872446130">.mw-parser-output .smallcaps{font-variant:small-caps}</style><span class="smallcaps">Rex armeniis Datus</span>: Lucius sat on a throne with his staff while Sohaemus stood before him, saluting the emperor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-257"><a href="#cite_note-257">[241]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 163, the Parthians intervened in <a href="/wiki/Osroene" title="Osroene">Osroene</a>, a Roman client in upper Mesopotamia centred on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Edessa,_Mesopotamia" title="Edessa, Mesopotamia">Edessa</a>, and installed their own king on its throne.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-258"><a href="#cite_note-258">[242]</a></sup> In response, Roman forces were moved downstream, to cross the <a href="/wiki/Euphrates" title="Euphrates">Euphrates</a> at a more southerly point.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-259"><a href="#cite_note-259">[243]</a></sup> Before the end of 163, however, Roman forces had moved north to occupy Dausara and Nicephorium on the northern, Parthian bank.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-260"><a href="#cite_note-260">[244]</a></sup> Soon after the conquest of the north bank of the Euphrates, other Roman forces moved on Osroene from Armenia, taking Anthemusia, a town southwest of Edessa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-261"><a href="#cite_note-261">[245]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 165, Roman forces moved on Mesopotamia. Edessa was re-occupied, and Mannus, the king deposed by the Parthians, was re-installed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-262"><a href="#cite_note-262">[246]</a></sup> The Parthians retreated to Nisibis, but this too was besieged and captured. The Parthian army dispersed in the <a href="/wiki/Tigris" title="Tigris">Tigris</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-263"><a href="#cite_note-263">[247]</a></sup> A second force, under Avidius Cassius and the III Gallica, moved down the Euphrates, and fought a major battle at Dura.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-264"><a href="#cite_note-264">[248]</a></sup>\n</p><p>By the end of the year, Cassius\' army had reached the twin metropolises of Mesopotamia: <a href="/wiki/Seleucia" title="Seleucia">Seleucia</a> on the right bank of the Tigris and <a href="/wiki/Ctesiphon" title="Ctesiphon">Ctesiphon</a> on the left. Ctesiphon was taken and its royal palace set to flame. The citizens of Seleucia, still largely Greek (the city had been commissioned and settled as a capital of the <a href="/wiki/Seleucid_Empire" title="Seleucid Empire">Seleucid Empire</a>, one of <a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">Alexander the Great</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Diadochi" title="Diadochi">successor kingdoms</a>), opened its gates to the invaders. The city was sacked nonetheless, leaving a black mark on Lucius\' reputation. Excuses were sought, or invented: the official version had it that the Seleucids broke faith first.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-265"><a href="#cite_note-265">[249]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Cassius\' army, although suffering from a shortage of supplies and the effects of a plague contracted in Seleucia, made it back to Roman territory safely.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-266"><a href="#cite_note-266">[250]</a></sup> Lucius took the title Parthicus Maximus, and he and Marcus were hailed as <i>imperatores</i> again, earning the title \'imp. III\'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-267"><a href="#cite_note-267">[251]</a></sup> Cassius\' army returned to the field in 166, crossing over the Tigris into Media. Lucius took the title \'Medicus\',<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-268"><a href="#cite_note-268">[252]</a></sup> and the emperors were again hailed as <i>imperatores</i>, becoming \'imp. IV\' in imperial titulature. Marcus took the Parthicus Maximus now, after another tactful delay.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-269"><a href="#cite_note-269">[253]</a></sup> On 12 October of that year, Marcus proclaimed two of his sons, <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Annius_Verus_Caesar" title="Marcus Annius Verus Caesar">Annius</a> and <a href="/wiki/Commodus" title="Commodus">Commodus</a>, as <a href="/wiki/Caesar_(title)" title="Caesar (title)">his heirs</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_270-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto-270">[254]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="War_with_Germanic_tribes_.28166.E2.80.93180.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="War_with_Germanic_tribes_(166&#8211;180)">War with Germanic tribes (166&#8211;180)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Marcus_Aurelius&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: War with Germanic tribes (166&#8211;180)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Marcomannic_Wars" title="Marcomannic Wars">Marcomannic Wars</a></div>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" 2x" width="258"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Marcus Aurelius celebrating <a href="/wiki/Roman_triumph" title="Roman triumph">his triumph</a> over Rome\'s enemies in 176 AD, riding in a <i><a href="/wiki/Quadriga" title="Quadriga">quadriga</a></i> chariot</div></div><div style="clear:left"></div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Marcus_Aurelius,_AE_medallion,_AD_168,_Gnecchi_II_52.jpg"><img alt="Bronze medallion of Marcus Aurelius" class="thumbimage" //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/d8/Marcus_Aurelius%2C_AE_medallion%2C_AD_168%2C_Gnecchi_II_52.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Marcus_Aurelius,_AE_medallion,_AD_168,_Gnecchi_II_52.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bronze medallion of Marcus Aurelius (AD 168). The reverse depicts <a href="/wiki/Jupiter_(mythology)" title="Jupiter (mythology)">Jupiter</a>, flanked by Marcus and <a href="/wiki/Lucius_Verus" title="Lucius Verus">Lucius Verus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-271"><a href="#cite_note-271">[255]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Marcus_Aurelius,_aureus,_AD_161-180,_RIC_III_362.jpg"><img alt="Aureus of Marcus Aurelius" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Marcus_Aurelius%2C_aureus%2C_AD_161-180%2C_RIC_III_362.jpg/300px-Marcus_Aurelius%2C_aureus%2C_AD_161-180%2C_RIC_III_362.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Marcus_Aurelius%2C_aureus%2C_AD_161-180%2C_RIC_III_362.jpg/450px-Marcus_Aurelius%2C_aureus%2C_AD_161-180%2C_RIC_III_362.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Marcus_Aurelius%2C_aureus%2C_AD_161-180%2C_RIC_III_362.jpg/600px-Marcus_Aurelius%2C_aureus%2C_AD_161-180%2C_RIC_III_362.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Marcus_Aurelius,_aureus,_AD_161-180,_RIC_III_362.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Aureus" title="Aureus">Aureus</a> of Marcus Aurelius (AD 176-177). The pile of trophies on the reverse celebrates the end of the Marcomannic Wars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-272"><a href="#cite_note-272">[256]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>During the early 160s, Fronto\'s son-in-law Victorinus was stationed as a legate in Germany. He was there with his wife and children (another child had stayed with Fronto and his wife in Rome).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-273"><a href="#cite_note-273">[257]</a></sup> The condition on the northern frontier looked grave. A frontier post had been destroyed, and it looked like all the peoples of central and northern Europe were in turmoil. There was corruption among the officers: Victorinus had to ask for the resignation of a <a href="/wiki/Legionary" title="Legionary">legionary</a> legate who was taking bribes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-274"><a href="#cite_note-274">[258]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Experienced governors had been replaced by friends and relatives of the imperial family. <a href="/wiki/Lucius_Dasumius_Tullius_Tuscus" title="Lucius Dasumius Tullius Tuscus">Lucius Dasumius Tullius Tuscus</a>, a distant relative of Hadrian, was in Upper Pannonia, succeeding the experienced <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Nonius_Macrinus" title="Marcus Nonius Macrinus">Marcus Nonius Macrinus</a>. Lower Pannonia was under the obscure <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Tiberius_Haterius_Saturnius&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Tiberius Haterius Saturnius (page does not exist)">Tiberius Haterius Saturnius</a>. <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Servilius_Fabianus_Maximus" title="Marcus Servilius Fabianus Maximus">Marcus Servilius Fabianus Maximus</a> was shuffled from Lower Moesia to Upper Moesia when <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marcus_Iallius_Bassus" title="Marcus Iallius Bassus">Marcus Iallius Bassus</a> had joined Lucius in Antioch. Lower Moesia was filled by Pontius Laelianus\' son. The Dacias were still divided in three, governed by a praetorian senator and two procurators. The peace could not hold long; Lower Pannonia did not even have a legion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-275"><a href="#cite_note-275">[259]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Starting in the 160s, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Germanic_tribes" title="Germanic tribes">Germanic tribes</a> and other nomadic people launched raids along the <a href="/wiki/Limes_Germanicus" title="Limes Germanicus">northern border</a>, particularly into <a href="/wiki/Gaul" title="Gaul">Gaul</a> and across the <a href="/wiki/Danube#Human_history" title="Danube">Danube</a>. This new impetus westwards was probably due to attacks from tribes further east. A first invasion of the Chatti in the province of Germania Superior was repulsed in 162.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McLynn2010_276-0"><a href="#cite_note-McLynn2010-276">[260]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Far more dangerous was the invasion of 166, when the <a href="/wiki/Marcomanni" title="Marcomanni">Marcomanni</a> of Bohemia, clients of the Roman Empire since 19 AD, crossed the Danube together with the <a href="/wiki/Lombards" title="Lombards">Lombards</a> and other Germanic tribes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bohec2013_277-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bohec2013-277">[261]</a></sup> Soon thereafter, the Iranian <a href="/wiki/Sarmatians" title="Sarmatians">Sarmatians</a> attacked between the Danube and the <a href="/wiki/Tisza" title="Tisza">Theiss</a> rivers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grant2016_278-0"><a href="#cite_note-Grant2016-278">[262]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Costoboci" title="Costoboci">Costoboci</a>, coming from the <a href="/wiki/Carpathian_Mountains" title="Carpathian Mountains">Carpathian</a> area, invaded <a href="/wiki/Moesia" title="Moesia">Moesia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Macedonia_(Roman_province)" title="Macedonia (Roman province)">Macedonia</a>, and Greece. After a long struggle, Marcus managed to push back the invaders. Numerous members of Germanic tribes settled in frontier regions like <a href="/wiki/Dacia" title="Dacia">Dacia</a>, Pannonia, Germany, and Italy itself. This was not a new thing, but this time the numbers of settlers required the creation of two new frontier provinces on the left shore of the Danube, Sarmatia and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marcomannia" title="Marcomannia">Marcomannia</a>, including today\'s <a href="/wiki/Czech_Republic" title="Czech Republic">Czech Republic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Slovakia" title="Slovakia">Slovakia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hungary" title="Hungary">Hungary</a>. Some Germanic tribes who settled in <a href="/wiki/Ravenna" title="Ravenna">Ravenna</a> revolted and managed to seize possession of the city. For this reason, Marcus decided not only against bringing more barbarians into Italy, but even banished those who had previously been brought there.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-279"><a href="#cite_note-279">[263]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Legal_and_administrative_work">Legal and administrative work</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Marcus_Aurelius&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Legal and administrative work">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Portrait_de_Marc_Aur%C3%A8le_2.jpg"><img alt="Bust of Marcus Aurelius" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4308" data-file-width="2872" decoding="async" height="330" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Portrait_de_Marc_Aur%C3%A8le_2.jpg/220px-Portrait_de_Marc_Aur%C3%A8le_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Portrait_de_Marc_Aur%C3%A8le_2.jpg/330px-Portrait_de_Marc_Aur%C3%A8le_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Portrait_de_Marc_Aur%C3%A8le_2.jpg/440px-Portrait_de_Marc_Aur%C3%A8le_2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Portrait_de_Marc_Aur%C3%A8le_2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bust of Marcus Aurelius in the <a href="/wiki/Liebieghaus" title="Liebieghaus">Liebieghaus</a>, Frankfurt</div></div></div>\n<p>Like many emperors, Marcus spent most of his time addressing matters of law such as petitions and hearing disputes,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-280"><a href="#cite_note-280">[264]</a></sup> but unlike many of his predecessors, he was already proficient in imperial administration when he assumed power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-281"><a href="#cite_note-281">[265]</a></sup> He took great care in the theory and practice of legislation. Professional jurists called him \'an emperor most skilled in the law\'<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-282"><a href="#cite_note-282">[266]</a></sup> and \'a most prudent and conscientiously just emperor\'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-283"><a href="#cite_note-283">[267]</a></sup> He showed marked interest in three areas of the law: the manumission of slaves, the guardianship of orphans and minors, and the choice of city councillors (<i>decuriones</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-284"><a href="#cite_note-284">[268]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Marcus showed a great deal of respect to the Roman Senate and routinely asked them for permission to spend money even though he did not need to do so as the absolute ruler of the Empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-285"><a href="#cite_note-285">[269]</a></sup> In one speech, Marcus himself reminded the Senate that the imperial palace where he lived was not truly his possession but theirs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-286"><a href="#cite_note-286">[270]</a></sup> In 168, he revalued the denarius, increasing the silver purity from 79% to 82%&#8212;the actual silver weight increasing from 2.57 grams to 2.67 grams. However, two years later he reverted to the previous values because of the military crises facing the empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TulaneUniversity_168-1"><a href="#cite_note-TulaneUniversity-168">[158]</a></sup>\n</p><p>A possible contact with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Han_Dynasty" title="Han Dynasty">Han China</a> occurred in 166 when a <a href="/wiki/Sino-Roman_relations" title="Sino-Roman relations">Roman traveller visited the Han court</a>, claiming to be an ambassador representing a certain Andun (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E5%AE%89" title="wikt:&#23433;">&#23433;</a><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E6%95%A6" title="wikt:&#25958;">&#25958;</a>), ruler of <a href="/wiki/Daqin" title="Daqin">Daqin</a>, who can be identified either with Marcus or his predecessor Antoninus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-287"><a href="#cite_note-287">[271]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-288"><a href="#cite_note-288">[272]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-289"><a href="#cite_note-289">[273]</a></sup> In addition to <a href="/wiki/Roman_Republic" title="Roman Republic">Republican</a>-era <a href="/wiki/Roman_glass" title="Roman glass">Roman glasswares</a> found at <a href="/wiki/Guangzhou" title="Guangzhou">Guangzhou</a> along the <a href="/wiki/South_China_Sea" title="South China Sea">South China Sea</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-290"><a href="#cite_note-290">[274]</a></sup> Roman golden medallions made during the reign of Antoninus and perhaps even Marcus have been found at <a href="/wiki/%C3%93c_Eo" title="&#211;c Eo">&#211;c Eo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a>, then part of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Funan" title="Kingdom of Funan">Kingdom of Funan</a> near the Chinese province of <a href="/wiki/Jiaozhi" title="Jiaozhi">Jiaozhi</a> (in northern Vietnam). This may have been the port city of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kattigara" title="Kattigara">Kattigara</a>, described by <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy" title="Ptolemy">Ptolemy</a> (c. 150) as being visited by a Greek sailor named Alexander and laying beyond the <a href="/wiki/Golden_Chersonese" title="Golden Chersonese">Golden Chersonese</a> (i.e. <a href="/wiki/Malay_Peninsula" title="Malay Peninsula">Malay Peninsula</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-young_2001_pp._29-30_291-0"><a href="#cite_note-young_2001_pp._29-30-291">[275]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-292"><a href="#cite_note-292">[notes 17]</a></sup> Roman coins from the reigns of <a href="/wiki/Tiberius" title="Tiberius">Tiberius</a> to <a href="/wiki/Aurelian" title="Aurelian">Aurelian</a> have been found in <a href="/wiki/Xi%27an" title="Xi\'an">Xi\'an</a>, China (site of the Han capital <a href="/wiki/Chang%27an" title="Chang\'an">Chang\'an</a>), although the far greater amount of <a href="/wiki/Indo-Roman_trade_relations" title="Indo-Roman trade relations">Roman coins in India</a> suggests the Roman maritime trade for <a href="/wiki/History_of_silk" title="History of silk">purchasing Chinese silk</a> was centred there, not in China or even the overland <a href="/wiki/Silk_Road" title="Silk Road">Silk Road</a> running through Persia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ball_2016_p154_293-0"><a href="#cite_note-ball_2016_p154-293">[276]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Plague">Plague</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Marcus_Aurelius&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Plague">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Antonine_Plague" title="Antonine Plague">Antonine Plague</a></div>\n<p>When Lucius\' army returned from the war with Parthia in 166, it brought with it <a href="/wiki/Antonine_Plague" title="Antonine Plague">plague</a>. Cases of plague occurred throughout the Empire for years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-294"><a href="#cite_note-294">[277]</a></sup> It caused the deaths of a quarter of those it infected, in total five million people. Galen mentions that \'fever, diarrhoea, and inflammation of the pharynx, along with dry or pustular eruptions of the skin after nine days\' were among the symptoms, and scholars have concluded the <a href="/wiki/Pandemic" title="Pandemic">pandemic</a> to have been <a href="/wiki/Smallpox" title="Smallpox">smallpox</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-murphy_295-0"><a href="#cite_note-murphy-295">[278]</a></sup> 19th century medical author <a href="/wiki/Heinrich_H%C3%A4ser" title="Heinrich H&#228;ser">Heinrich H&#228;ser</a> believes that it may have been either smallpox or <a href="/wiki/Measles" title="Measles">measles</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-296"><a href="#cite_note-296">[279]</a></sup> However, historian J. F. Gilliam concluded in his summary of the written sources, with inconclusive Greek and Latin inscriptions, two groups of papyri and coinage: \'There is not enough evidence satisfactorily to identify the disease or diseases\'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-297"><a href="#cite_note-297">[280]</a></sup> The plague struck again between 251 and 266. It may have killed up to 5,000 people per day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-murphy_295-1"><a href="#cite_note-murphy-295">[278]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Death_and_succession_.28180.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Death_and_succession_(180)">Death and succession (180)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Marcus_Aurelius&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Death and succession (180)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Delacroix-Marc_Aur%C3%A8le-MBA-Lyon.jpg"><img alt="Painting that depicts Marcus on his deathbed and his son Commodus, surrounded by the emperor\'s philosopher friends" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1173" data-file-width="1500" decoding="async" height="203" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Delacroix-Marc_Aur%C3%A8le-MBA-Lyon.jpg/260px-Delacroix-Marc_Aur%C3%A8le-MBA-Lyon.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Delacroix-Marc_Aur%C3%A8le-MBA-Lyon.jpg/390px-Delacroix-Marc_Aur%C3%A8le-MBA-Lyon.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Delacroix-Marc_Aur%C3%A8le-MBA-Lyon.jpg/520px-Delacroix-Marc_Aur%C3%A8le-MBA-Lyon.jpg 2x" width="260"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Delacroix-Marc_Aur%C3%A8le-MBA-Lyon.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Last_Words_of_the_Emperor_Marcus_Aurelius" title="Last Words of the Emperor Marcus Aurelius">Last Words of the Emperor Marcus Aurelius</a></i> (1844) by <a href="/wiki/Eug%C3%A8ne_Delacroix" title="Eug&#232;ne Delacroix">Eug&#232;ne Delacroix</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Marcus died on 17 March 180 due to natural causes in the city of Vindobona (modern Vienna). He was immediately deified and his ashes were returned to Rome, where they rested in Hadrian\'s <a href="/wiki/Mausoleum" title="Mausoleum">mausoleum</a> (modern <a href="/wiki/Castel_Sant%27Angelo" title="Castel Sant\'Angelo">Castel Sant\'Angelo</a>) until the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Visigoth" title="Visigoth">Visigoth</a> <a href="/wiki/Sack_of_Rome_(410)" title="Sack of Rome (410)">sack of the city</a> in 410. His campaigns against Germans and Sarmatians were also commemorated by a <a href="/wiki/Column_of_Marcus_Aurelius" title="Column of Marcus Aurelius">column</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Temple_of_Marcus_Aurelius" title="Temple of Marcus Aurelius">temple</a> built in Rome.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kleiner2016_298-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kleiner2016-298">[281]</a></sup> Some scholars consider his death to be the end of the <a href="/wiki/Pax_Romana" title="Pax Romana">Pax Romana</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Merrony2017_299-0"><a href="#cite_note-Merrony2017-299">[282]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Marcus was succeeded by his son Commodus, whom he had named Caesar in 166 and with whom he had jointly ruled since 177.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Birley186&#8211;91_300-0"><a href="#cite_note-Birley186&#8211;91-300">[283]</a></sup> Biological sons of the emperor, if there were any, were considered heirs;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-301"><a href="#cite_note-301">[284]</a></sup> however, it was only the second time that a \'non-adoptive\' son had succeeded his father, the only other having been a century earlier when <a href="/wiki/Vespasian" title="Vespasian">Vespasian</a> was succeeded by his son Titus. Historians have criticized the succession to Commodus, citing Commodus\' erratic behaviour and lack of political and military acumen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Birley186&#8211;91_300-1"><a href="#cite_note-Birley186&#8211;91-300">[283]</a></sup> At the end of his history of Marcus\' reign, Cassius Dio wrote an <a href="/wiki/Encomium" title="Encomium">encomium</a> to the emperor, and described the transition to Commodus in his own lifetime with sorrow:\n</p>\n<blockquote><p>[Marcus] did not meet with the good fortune that he deserved, for he was not strong in body and was involved in a multitude of troubles throughout practically his entire reign. But for my part, I admire him all the more for this very reason, that amid unusual and extraordinary difficulties he both survived himself and preserved the empire. Just one thing prevented him from being completely happy, namely, that after rearing and educating his son in the best possible way he was vastly disappointed in him. This matter must be our next topic; for our history now descends from a kingdom of gold to one of iron and rust, as affairs did for the Romans of that day.\n</p><dl><dd>&#8211;Dio lxxi. 36.3&#8211;4<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-302"><a href="#cite_note-302">[285]</a></sup></dd></dl></blockquote>\n<p>Dio adds that from Marcus\' first days as counsellor to Antoninius to his final days as emperor of Rome, \'he remained the same [person] and did not change in the least.\'<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-303"><a href="#cite_note-303">[286]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Michael_Grant_(classicist)" title="Michael Grant (classicist)">Michael Grant</a>, in <i>The Climax of Rome</i>, writes of Commodus:\n</p>\n<blockquote><p>The youth turned out to be very erratic, or at least so anti-traditional that disaster was inevitable. But whether or not Marcus ought to have known this to be so, the rejections of his son\'s claims in favour of someone else would almost certainly have involved one of the civil wars which were to proliferate so disastrously around future successions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grant2011_304-0"><a href="#cite_note-Grant2011-304">[287]</a></sup></p></blockquote>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy_and_reputation">Legacy and reputation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Marcus_Aurelius&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Legacy and reputation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Marcus acquired the reputation of a <a href="/wiki/Philosopher_king" title="Philosopher king">philosopher king</a> within his lifetime, and the title would remain his after death; both Dio and the biographer call him \'the philosopher\'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-305"><a href="#cite_note-305">[288]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-306"><a href="#cite_note-306">[289]</a></sup> Christians such as <a href="/wiki/Justin_Martyr" title="Justin Martyr">Justin Martyr</a>, Athenagoras, and Melito also gave him the title.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-307"><a href="#cite_note-307">[290]</a></sup> The last named went so far as to call him \'more philanthropic and philosophic\' than Antoninus and Hadrian, and set him against the persecuting emperors Domitian and Nero to make the contrast bolder.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-308"><a href="#cite_note-308">[291]</a></sup> \'Alone of the emperors,\' wrote the historian Herodian, \'he gave proof of his learning not by mere words or knowledge of philosophical doctrines but by his blameless character and temperate way of life\'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-309"><a href="#cite_note-309">[292]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Iain_King" title="Iain King">Iain King</a> concludes that Marcus\' legacy is tragic, because the emperor\'s \'Stoic philosophy&#8212;which is about self-restraint, duty, and respect for others&#8212;was so abjectly abandoned by the imperial line he anointed on his death\'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-310"><a href="#cite_note-310">[293]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Attitude_towards_Christians">Attitude towards Christians</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Marcus_Aurelius&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Attitude towards Christians">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>In the first two centuries of the Christian era, it was local Roman officials who were largely responsible for the <a href="/wiki/Persecution_of_Christians_in_the_Roman_Empire" title="Persecution of Christians in the Roman Empire">persecution of Christians</a>. In the second century, the emperors treated Christianity as a local problem to be dealt with by their subordinates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-311"><a href="#cite_note-311">[294]</a></sup> The number and severity of persecutions of Christians in various locations of the empire seemingly increased during the reign of Marcus. The extent to which Marcus himself directed, encouraged, or was aware of these persecutions is unclear and much debated by historians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McLynn2010295_312-0"><a href="#cite_note-McLynn2010295-312">[295]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Marriage_and_children"><span id="Marriage_and_issue"></span>Marriage and children</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Marcus_Aurelius&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Marriage and children">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:492px;max-width:492px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:279px;max-width:279px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:279px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Commodus-AnniusVerus_tarsos_161-165_AE17_CNG.jpg"><img alt="Coin of Marcus\' sons Commodus and Annius facing each other" data-file-height="198" data-file-width="196" decoding="async" height="280" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/b4/Commodus-AnniusVerus_tarsos_161-165_AE17_CNG.jpg" width="277"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Coin of <a href="/wiki/Commodus" title="Commodus">Commodus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Annius_Verus_Caesar" title="Marcus Annius Verus Caesar">Annius</a>, 161&#8211;165</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:209px;max-width:209px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:279px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Busto_de_Vibia_Sabina_(M._Prado)_01.jpg"><img alt="Bust of Vibia Aurelia Sabina, Marcus\' daughter" data-file-height="1470" data-file-width="1090" decoding="async" height="279" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Busto_de_Vibia_Sabina_%28M._Prado%29_01.jpg/207px-Busto_de_Vibia_Sabina_%28M._Prado%29_01.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Busto_de_Vibia_Sabina_%28M._Prado%29_01.jpg/311px-Busto_de_Vibia_Sabina_%28M._Prado%29_01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Busto_de_Vibia_Sabina_%28M._Prado%29_01.jpg/414px-Busto_de_Vibia_Sabina_%28M._Prado%29_01.jpg 2x" width="207"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Bust of <a href="/wiki/Vibia_Aurelia_Sabina" title="Vibia Aurelia Sabina">Vibia Aurelia Sabina</a>, <a href="/wiki/Museo_del_Prado" title="Museo del Prado">Prado Museum</a></div></div><div style="clear:left"></div></div></div>\n<p>Marcus and his <a href="/wiki/Cousin_marriage" title="Cousin marriage">cousin-wife</a> Faustina had at least 13 children during their 30-year marriage,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._103_127-1"><a href="#cite_note-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._103-127">[120]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stephens,_p._31_313-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stephens,_p._31-313">[296]</a></sup> including two sets of twins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._103_127-2"><a href="#cite_note-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._103-127">[120]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-livius3_314-0"><a href="#cite_note-livius3-314">[297]</a></sup> One son and four daughters outlived their father.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-315"><a href="#cite_note-315">[298]</a></sup> Their children included:\n</p>\n<ul><li>Domitia Faustina (147 &#8211; 151)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._103_127-3"><a href="#cite_note-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._103-127">[120]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-livius1_139-1"><a Birley (1997), <i>Hadrian, the Restless Emperor</i>, p. 251, cited in Levick (2014), p. 30, who is sceptical of this suggestion.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-336"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-336">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Husband of Rupilia Faustina: Levick (2014), p. 163.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-lev163-337"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-lev163_337-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-lev163_337-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-lev163_337-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-lev163_337-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">Levick (2014), p. 163.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-lev162-338"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-lev162_338-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-lev162_338-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-lev162_338-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-lev162_338-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">Levick (2014), p. 162.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-lev164-339"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-lev164_339-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-lev164_339-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-lev164_339-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-lev164_339-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-lev164_339-4"><sup><i><b>e</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-lev164_339-5"><sup><i><b>f</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-lev164_339-6"><sup><i><b>g</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">Levick (2014), p. 164.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-340"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-340">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Wife of M. This is also echoed in <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Historia_Augusta" title="Historia Augusta">HA</a></i> <a class="external text" href="http://penelope.uchicago.edu/Thayer/E/Roman/Texts/Historia_Augusta/Marcus_Aurelius/2*.html" rel="nofollow">"Marcus Aurelius" 24</a>.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-344"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-344">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Husband of Ceionia Fabia: Levick (2014), p. 164.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-lev117-345"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-lev117_345-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-lev117_345-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-lev117_345-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">Levick (2014), p. 117.</span>\n</li>\n</ol></div>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td style="text-align: left;"><b>References:</b>\n<ul><li><cite class="citation web"><i>DIR</i> contributors (2000). <a class="external text" href="http://www.roman-emperors.org/" rel="nofollow">"De Imperatoribus Romanis: An Online Encyclopedia of Roman Rulers and Their Families"</a><span class="reference-accessdate">. Oxford University Press. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-19-537941-9" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-19-537941-9">978-0-19-537941-9</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Faustina+I+and+II%3A+Imperial+Women+of+the+Golden+Age&amp;rft.pub=Oxford+University+Press&amp;rft.date=2014&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-19-537941-9&amp;rft.aulast=Levick&amp;rft.aufirst=Barbara&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AMarcus+Aurelius"></span><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r879151008" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></li>\n<li><cite class="citation book"><a href="/wiki/William_Smith_(lexicographer)" title="William Smith (lexicographer)">William Smith</a>, ed. (1870). <i><a href="/wiki/Dictionary_of_Greek_and_Roman_Biography_and_Mythology" title="Dictionary of Greek and Roman Biography and Mythology">Dictionary of Greek and Roman Biography and Mythology</a></i>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Dictionary+of+Greek+and+Roman+Biography+and+Mythology&amp;rft.date=1870&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AMarcus+Aurelius"></span><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r879151008" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></li></ul>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Writings">Writings</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Marcus_Aurelius&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Writings">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Meditations" title="Meditations">Meditations</a></div>\n<p>While on campaign between 170 and 180, Marcus wrote his <i><a href="/wiki/Meditations" title="Meditations">Meditations</a></i> in Greek as a source for his own guidance and self-improvement. The original title of this work, if it had one, is unknown. \'Meditations\' &#8211; as well as other titles including \'To Himself\' &#8211; were adopted later. He had a logical mind and his notes were representative of Stoic philosophy and spirituality. <i>Meditations</i> is still revered as a literary monument to a government of service and duty. According to Hays, the book was a favourite of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christina_of_Sweden" title="Christina of Sweden">Christina of Sweden</a>, <a href="/wiki/Frederick_the_Great" title="Frederick the Great">Frederick the Great</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Stuart_Mill" title="John Stuart Mill">John Stuart Mill</a>, <a href="/wiki/Matthew_Arnold" title="Matthew Arnold">Matthew Arnold</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Goethe" title="Goethe">Goethe</a>, and is admired by modern figures such as <a href="/wiki/Wen_Jiabao" title="Wen Jiabao">Wen Jiabao</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bill_Clinton" title="Bill Clinton">Bill Clinton</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-346"><a href="#cite_note-346">[306]</a></sup> It has been considered by many commentators to be one of the greatest works of philosophy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Collins1973_347-0"><a href="#cite_note-Collins1973-347">[307]</a></sup>\n</p><p>It is not known how widely Marcus\' writings were circulated after his death. There are stray references in the ancient literature to the popularity of his precepts, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Julian_the_Apostate" title="Julian the Apostate">Julian the Apostate</a> was well aware of his reputation as a philosopher, though he does not specifically mention <i>Meditations</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-348"><a href="#cite_note-348">[308]</a></sup> It survived in the scholarly traditions of the Eastern Church and the first surviving quotes of the book, as well as the first known reference of it by name (\'Marcus\' writings to himself\') are from <a href="/wiki/Arethas_of_Caesarea" title="Arethas of Caesarea">Arethas of Caesarea</a> in the 10th century and in the Byzantine <a href="/wiki/Suda" title="Suda">Suda</a> (perhaps inserted by Arethas himself). It was first published in 1558 in Zurich by <a href="/wiki/Wilhelm_Xylander" title="Wilhelm Xylander">Wilhelm Xylander (ne Holzmann)</a>, from a manuscript reportedly lost shortly afterwards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-349"><a href="#cite_note-349">[309]</a></sup> The oldest surviving complete manuscript copy is in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vatican_library" title="Vatican library">Vatican library</a> and dates to the 14th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-350"><a href="#cite_note-350">[310]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Equestrian_Statue_of_Marcus_Aurelius">Equestrian Statue of Marcus Aurelius</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Marcus_Aurelius&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Equestrian Statue of Marcus Aurelius">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Equestrian_Statue_of_Marcus_Aurelius" title="Equestrian Statue of Marcus Aurelius">Equestrian Statue of Marcus Aurelius</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Marcus_Aurelius,_aureus,_AD_174,_RIC_III_295.jpg"><img alt="Aureus of Marcus Aurelius" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="343" data-file-width="736" decoding="async" height="140" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Marcus_Aurelius%2C_aureus%2C_AD_174%2C_RIC_III_295.jpg/300px-Marcus_Aurelius%2C_aureus%2C_AD_174%2C_RIC_III_295.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Marcus_Aurelius%2C_aureus%2C_AD_174%2C_RIC_III_295.jpg/450px-Marcus_Aurelius%2C_aureus%2C_AD_174%2C_RIC_III_295.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Marcus_Aurelius%2C_aureus%2C_AD_174%2C_RIC_III_295.jpg/600px-Marcus_Aurelius%2C_aureus%2C_AD_174%2C_RIC_III_295.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Marcus_Aurelius,_aureus,_AD_174,_RIC_III_295.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Aureus" title="Aureus">Aureus</a> of Marcus Aurelius (AD December 173- June 174), with his equestrian statue on the reverse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-351"><a href="#cite_note-351">[311]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Marc_Aurel_column_detailed_view_01.jpg"><img alt="Column of Marcus Aurelius" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="20782" data-file-width="5152" decoding="async" height="887" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Marc_Aurel_column_detailed_view_01.jpg/220px-Marc_Aurel_column_detailed_view_01.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Marc_Aurel_column_detailed_view_01.jpg/330px-Marc_Aurel_column_detailed_view_01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Marc_Aurel_column_detailed_view_01.jpg/440px-Marc_Aurel_column_detailed_view_01.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Marc_Aurel_column_detailed_view_01.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Detailed view of the <a href="/wiki/Column_of_Marcus_Aurelius" title="Column of Marcus Aurelius">Column of Marcus Aurelius</a></div></div></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Equestrian_Statue_of_Marcus_Aurelius" title="Equestrian Statue of Marcus Aurelius">Equestrian Statue of Marcus Aurelius</a> in Rome is the only Roman equestrian statue which has survived into the modern period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-352"><a href="#cite_note-352">[312]</a></sup> This is perhaps due to it being wrongly identified during the Middle Ages as a depiction of the <a href="/wiki/Christianity_in_the_4th_century" title="Christianity in the 4th century">Christian emperor</a> <a href="/wiki/Constantine_the_Great" title="Constantine the Great">Constantine the Great</a> and hence spared destruction, unlike some other statues associated with <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_ancient_Rome" title="Religion in ancient Rome">paganism</a>. Crafted of bronze in circa 175 AD, it stands 11.6&#160;ft (3.5&#160;m) and is now located in the <a href="/wiki/Capitoline_Museums" title="Capitoline Museums">Capitoline Museums</a> of Rome. The emperor\'s hand is outstretched in an act of clemency offered to a bested enemy, while his weary facial expression due to the stress of leading Rome into nearly constant battles perhaps represents a break with the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_art" title="Ancient Greek art">classical tradition</a> of <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_sculpture" title="Ancient Greek sculpture">sculpture</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-353"><a href="#cite_note-353">[313]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional">\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px"><div style="width: 155px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 150px;"><div style="margin:31.5px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Marco_Aurelio_bronzo.JPG"><img alt="" data-file-height="1954" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Marco_Aurelio_bronzo.JPG/180px-Marco_Aurelio_bronzo.JPG 1.5x, class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px"><div style="width: 155px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 150px;"><div style="margin:15px auto;"><a class="image"
