width="85"/></a></div></div>\n<div class="gallerytext">\n<p>Replica of the statue, <a href="/wiki/Capitoline_Hill" title="Capitoline Hill">Capitoline Hill</a>\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n</ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Column_of_Marcus_Aurelius">Column of Marcus Aurelius</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Marcus_Aurelius&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Column of Marcus Aurelius">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Column_of_Marcus_Aurelius" title="Column of Marcus Aurelius">Column of Marcus Aurelius</a></div>\n<p>Marcus\' <a href="/wiki/Victory_column" title="Victory column">victory column</a>, established in Rome either in his last few years of life or after his reign and completed in 193 AD, was built to commemorate his victory over the Sarmatians and Germanic tribes in 176 AD. A spiral of carved <a href="/wiki/Relief" title="Relief">reliefs</a> wraps around the column, showing scenes from his military campaigns. A statue of Marcus had stood atop the column but disappeared during the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>. It was replaced with a statue of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saint_Paul" title="Saint Paul">Saint Paul</a> in 1589 by pope <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sixtus_V" title="Sixtus V">Sixtus V</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-354"><a href="#cite_note-354">[314]</a></sup> The column of Marcus and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Column_of_Trajan" title="Column of Trajan">column of Trajan</a> are often compared by scholars given how they are both <a href="/wiki/Doric_order" title="Doric order">Doric</a> in style, had a pedestal at the base, had sculpted <a href="/wiki/Frieze" title="Frieze">friezes</a> depicting their respective military victories, and a statue on top.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-355"><a href="#cite_note-355">[315]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional">\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px"><div style="width: 155px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 150px;"><div style="margin:15px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2006_Piazza_Colonna_-_panoramio.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1000" data-file-width="664" decoding="async" height="120" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/2006_Piazza_Colonna_-_panoramio.jpg/80px-2006_Piazza_Colonna_-_panoramio.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/2006_Piazza_Colonna_-_panoramio.jpg/119px-2006_Piazza_Colonna_-_panoramio.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/2006_Piazza_Colonna_-_panoramio.jpg/159px-2006_Piazza_Colonna_-_panoramio.jpg 2x" width="80"/></a></div></div>\n<div class="gallerytext">\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Column_of_Marcus_Aurelius" title="Column of Marcus Aurelius">Column of Marcus Aurelius</a> in <a href="/wiki/Piazza_Colonna" title="Piazza Colonna">Piazza Colonna</a>. The five horizontal slits allow light into the internal <a href="/wiki/List_of_ancient_spiral_stairs" title="List of ancient spiral stairs">spiral staircase</a>.\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px"><div style="width: 155px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 150px;"><div style="margin:37px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Montecitorio_Panini.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="364" data-file-width="574" decoding="async" height="76" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Montecitorio_Panini.jpg/120px-Montecitorio_Panini.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Montecitorio_Panini.jpg/180px-Montecitorio_Panini.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Montecitorio_Panini.jpg/240px-Montecitorio_Panini.jpg 2x" width="120"/></a></div></div>\n<div class="gallerytext">\n<p>The column, right, in the background of <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Paolo_Panini" title="Giovanni Paolo Panini">Panini\'s</a> painting of the <a href="/wiki/Palazzo_Montecitorio" title="Palazzo Montecitorio">Palazzo Montecitorio</a>, with the base of the <a href="/wiki/Column_of_Antoninus_Pius" title="Column of Antoninus Pius">Column of Antoninus Pius</a> in the right foreground (1747)\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n</ul>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Marcus_Aurelius&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;">\n<div class="mw-references-wrap mw-references-columns"><ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-24"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-24">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Dio asserts that the Annii were near-kin of Hadrian, and that it was to these familial ties that they owed their rise to power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> The precise nature of these kinship ties is nowhere stated, but is believed that <a href="/wiki/Rupilia" title="Rupilia">Rupilia Faustina</a> was the daughter of the consular senator <a href="/wiki/Libo_Rupilius_Frugi" title="Libo Rupilius Frugi">Libo Rupilius Frugi</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Salonina_Matidia" title="Salonina Matidia">Matidia</a>, who was also the mother (presumably through another marriage) of <a href="/wiki/Vibia_Sabina" title="Vibia Sabina">Vibia Sabina</a>, Hadrian\'s wife.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-31"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-31">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Farquharson dates his death to 130, when Marcus was nine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[29]</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-46"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-46">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Birley amends the text of the <i>HA Marcus</i> from \'Eutychius\' to \'Tuticius\'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[43]</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-57"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-57">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Commodus was a known consumptive at the time of his adoption, so Hadrian may have intended Marcus\' eventual succession anyway.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[53]</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-82"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-82">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The manuscript is corrupt here.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._89_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._89-80">[76]</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-101"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-101">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Modern scholars have not offered as positive an assessment. His second modern editor, <a href="/wiki/Barthold_Georg_Niebuhr" title="Barthold Georg Niebuhr">Niebhur</a>, thought him stupid and frivolous; his third editor, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Samuel_Adrian_Naber&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Samuel Adrian Naber (page does not exist)">Naber</a>, found him contemptible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[93]</a></sup> Historians have seen him as a \'pedant and a bore\', his letters offering neither the running political analysis of a Cicero or the conscientious reportage of a Pliny.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[94]</a></sup> Recent prosopographic research has rehabilitated his reputation, though not by much.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[95]</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-122"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-122">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Champlin notes that Marcus\' praise of Rusticus in the <i>Meditations</i> is out of order (he is praised immediately after Diognetus, who had introduced Marcus to philosophy), giving him special emphasis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[115]</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-144"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-144">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Although part of the biographer\'s account of Lucius is fictionalized (probably to mimic Nero, whose birthday Lucius shared<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[134]</a></sup>) and another part poorly compiled from a better biographical source,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[135]</a></sup> scholars have accepted these biographical details as accurate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[136]</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-162"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-162">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">These name-swaps have proven so confusing that even the <i>Historia Augusta</i>, our main source for the period, cannot keep them straight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[151]</a></sup> The 4th-century ecclesiastical historian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eusebius_of_Caesarea" title="Eusebius of Caesarea">Eusebius of Caesarea</a> shows even more confusion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[152]</a></sup> The mistaken belief that Lucius had the name \'Verus\' before becoming emperor has proven especially popular.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._117_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._117-161">[153]</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-163"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-163">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">There was, however, much precedent. The consulate was a twin magistracy, and earlier emperors had often had a subordinate lieutenant with many imperial offices (under Antoninus, the lieutenant had been Marcus). Many emperors had planned a joint succession in the past: <a href="/wiki/Augustus" title="Augustus">Augustus</a> planned to leave <a href="/wiki/Gaius_Caesar" title="Gaius Caesar">Gaius</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lucius_Caesar" title="Lucius Caesar">Lucius Caesar</a> as joint emperors on his death; Tiberius wished to have <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gaius_Caligula" title="Gaius Caligula">Gaius Caligula</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tiberius_Gemellus" title="Tiberius Gemellus">Tiberius Gemellus</a> do so as well; <a href="/wiki/Claudius" title="Claudius">Claudius</a> left the empire to <a href="/wiki/Nero" title="Nero">Nero</a> and <a href="/wiki/Britannicus" title="Britannicus">Britannicus</a>, imagining that they would accept equal rank. All of these arrangements had ended in failure, either through premature death (Gaius and Lucius Caesar) or judicial murder (Gemellus by Caligula and Britannicus by Nero).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._117_161-2"><a href="#cite_note-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._117-161">[153]</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-176"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-176">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The biographer relates the scurrilous (and, in the judgment of Anthony Birley, untrue) rumor that Commodus was an illegitimate child born of a union between Faustina and a gladiator.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[165]</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-193"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-193">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Because both Lucius and Marcus are said to have taken active part in the recovery (<i>HA Marcus</i> viii. 4&#8211;5), the flood must have happened before Lucius\' departure for the east in 162; because it appears in the biographer\'s narrative after Antoninus\' funeral has finished and the emperors have settled into their offices, it must not have occurred in the spring of 161. A date in autumn 161 or spring 162 is probable, and, given the normal seasonal distribution of Tiber flooding, the most probable date is in spring 162.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192"><a href="#cite_note-192">[181]</a></sup> (Birley dates the flood to autumn 161.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._120_187-1"><a href="#cite_note-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._120-187">[176]</a></sup>)</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-196"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-196">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Since 15 AD, the river had been administered by a Tiber Conservancy Board, with a consular senator at its head and a permanent staff. In 161, the <i>curator alevi Tiberis et riparum et cloacarum urbis</i> (\'Curator of the Tiber Bed and Banks and the City Sewers\') was A. Platorius Nepos, son or grandson of <a href="/wiki/Aulus_Platorius_Nepos" title="Aulus Platorius Nepos">the builder</a> of <a href="/wiki/Hadrian%27s_Wall" title="Hadrian\'s Wall">Hadrian\'s Wall</a>, whose name he shares. He probably had not been particularly incompetent. A more likely candidate for that incompetence is Nepos\' likely predecessor, <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Statius_Priscus" title="Marcus Statius Priscus">M. Statius Priscus</a>. A military man and consul for 159, Priscus probably looked on the office as little more than \'paid leave\'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195"><a href="#cite_note-195">[183]</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-212"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-212">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Alan_Cameron_(classical_scholar)" title="Alan Cameron (classical scholar)">Alan Cameron</a> adduces the 5th-century writer <a href="/wiki/Sidonius_Apollinaris" title="Sidonius Apollinaris">Sidonius Apollinaris</a>\'s comment that Marcus commanded \'countless legions\' <i>vivente Pio</i> (while Antoninus was alive) while contesting Birley\'s contention that Marcus had no military experience. (Neither Apollinaris nor the <i>Historia Augusta</i> (Birley\'s source) are particularly reliable on 2nd-century history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-211"><a href="#cite_note-211">[198]</a></sup>)</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-235"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-235">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Birley believes there is some truth in these considerations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-234"><a href="#cite_note-234">[220]</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-242"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-242">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The whole section of the <i>vita</i> dealing with Lucius\' debaucheries (<i>HA Verus</i> iv. 4&#8211;6.6), however, is an insertion into a narrative otherwise entirely cribbed from an earlier source. Most of the details are fabricated by the biographer himself, relying on nothing better than his own imagination.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-241"><a href="#cite_note-241">[226]</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-292"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-292">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">For further information on <a href="/wiki/%C3%93c_Eo" title="&#211;c Eo">&#211;c Eo</a>, see Osborne, Milton. <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=uxF2kH04WKgC" rel="nofollow"><i>The Mekong: Turbulent Past, Uncertain Future</i></a>. Crows Nest: Allen &amp; Unwin, 2006, revised edition, first published in 2000. pp. 24&#8211;25. <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r879151008" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9781741148930" title="Special:BookSources/9781741148930">9781741148930</a>.</span>\n</li>\n</ol></div></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Citations">Citations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Marcus_Aurelius&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Citations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>All citations to the <i>Historia Augusta</i> are to individual biographies, and are marked with a <i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'</span>HA<span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'</span></i>. Citations to the works of Fronto are cross-referenced to C.R. Haines\' Loeb edition.\n</p>\n<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: decimal;">\n<ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-1">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a class="external text" href="https://art.thewalters.org/detail/10416" rel="nofollow">\'Portrait of the Emperor Marcus Aurelius\'</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Walters_Art_Museum" title="The Walters Art Museum">The Walters Art Museum</a>.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-2"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-2">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a class="external text" href="https://www.dictionary.com/browse/marcus-aurelius" rel="nofollow">\'Marcus Aurelius\'</a> <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20181228082840/https://www.dictionary.com/browse/marcus-aurelius" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> 28 December 2018 at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a>.
